Q1. In a triangle ABC, tan $\frac{A}{2}=\frac{5}{6}, \tan \frac{B}{2}=\frac{20}{37}$ then tan $\frac C 2$ is equal to
    (A) $\frac {100} {222}$
    (B) $\frac 2 5$   <-- CORRECT
    (C) $\frac {34} {37}$
    (D) $\frac 5 2$
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Given tan $\frac{A}{2}$ = $\frac{5}{6}$ $\Rightarrow$ cot $\frac{A}{2}$ = $\frac{6}{5}$, tan $\frac{B}{2}$ = $\frac{20}{37}$ $\Rightarrow$ cot $\frac{B}{2}$ = $\frac{37}{20}$ 
Given A, B and C are angles of a triangle 
Then, A + B + C = 180
$\Rightarrow$ $\frac{A+B+C}{2}$ = $\frac{180}{2}$ 
$\Rightarrow$ $\frac{A+B}{2}+\frac{C}{2}$ = 90
$\Rightarrow$ $\frac{C}{2}$ = $90-\frac{A+B}{2}$ 
Taking tan on both sides, we get
$\Rightarrow$ $\tan \frac{C}{2}$ = $\tan \left(90-\frac{A+B}{2}\right)$ 
$\Rightarrow$ $\tan \frac{C}{2}$ = $\cot \left(\frac{A+B}{2}\right)$ = $\cot \left(\frac{A}{2}+\frac{B}{2}\right)$ = $\frac{\cot \frac{A}{2} \cdot \cot \frac{B}{2}-1}{\cot \frac{A}{2}+\cot \frac{B}{2}}$ = $\frac{\frac{6}{5} \cdot \frac{37}{20}-1}{\frac{6}{5}+\frac{37}{20}}$ = $\frac{\frac{222}{100}-1}{\frac{305}{100}}$ = $\frac{\frac{122}{100}}{\frac{305}{100}}$ = $\frac{122}{305}$ = $\frac{2}{5}$

Q2. In a triangle ABC, if A = ${75^o}$ and B = ${45^o}$ then b + 2 c is equal to
    (A) 2a   <-- CORRECT
    (B) $\frac{1}{2}(a+b+c)$
    (C) a
    (D) a + b + c
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Given $A=75^{\circ} \text { and } B=45^{\circ}$
then $A+B+C=180^{\circ}$
$\Rightarrow 75+45+C=180$
$\Rightarrow$C=180-120=60
From sin e formula
$\frac{a}{\sin A}=\frac{b}{\sin B}=\frac{c}{\sin C}=k(\operatorname{lc} t)$
$\Rightarrow \frac{a}{\sin 75}=\frac{b}{\sin 45}=\frac{c}{\sin 60}=k$
Here
$\frac{a}{\frac{\sqrt{3}+1}{2 \sqrt{2}}}=k, \frac{b}{\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}}=k, \frac{c}{\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}}=k$ Now,
$\Rightarrow a=\left(\frac{\sqrt{3}+1}{2 \sqrt{2}}\right) k, b=\frac{k}{\sqrt{2}}, c=\frac{\sqrt{3} k}{2}$
$b+2 c=\frac{k}{\sqrt{2}}+2 \cdot \frac{\sqrt{3} k}{2}=\frac{2 k+2 \sqrt{3} k}{2 \sqrt{2}}$$=2\left(\frac{\sqrt{3}+1}{2 \sqrt{2}}\right) k=2 a$

Q3. The angles of a triangle are as 1 : 2 : 7, then ratio of greatest side to least side is
    (A) $(\sqrt 2 + 3):(2 - \sqrt 3 )$
    (B) $(\sqrt 2 + 1):(\sqrt 2 - 1)$
    (C) $(\sqrt 5 + 1):(\sqrt 5 - 1)$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $(\sqrt 3 + 1):(\sqrt 3 - 1)$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Let angles x, 2x and 7x.
Then, x + 2x + 7x = 180^o 
$\Rightarrow$ 10x = 180
$\Rightarrow$ x = $\frac{180}{10}$ = 18^o 
From sin formula
$\frac{a}{\sin A}=\frac{b}{\sin B}=\frac{c}{\sin C}$ = k(let)
$\Rightarrow$ $\frac{a}{\sin 18}=\frac{b}{\sin 36}=\frac{c}{\sin 126}$ = k
Here a = k sin 18, b = k sin 36, c = k sin 126 
$\Rightarrow$ a = $\frac{\sqrt{5}-1}{4} \cdot k$, b = $\frac{\sqrt{10-2 \sqrt{5}}}{4} \cdot k$, c = $\frac{\sqrt{5}+1}{4} \cdot k$ 
a : b = $\sqrt{5}+1: \sqrt{5}-1$

Q4. If the sides of a triangle are 13, 7, 8 the greatest angle of the triangle is
    (A) $\frac {2\pi }{3}$   <-- CORRECT
    (B) $\frac {\pi}{ 2}$
    (C) $\frac {3\pi }{2}$
    (D) $\frac {\pi}{3}$
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Let the sides of the triangle be a = 13, b = 7 and c = 8.
Since a is the longest side the greatest angle is A.
Using Cosine rule, we have
cos A = $\frac{b^{2}+c^{2}-a^{2}}{2 b c}$ = $\frac{49+64-169}{112}$ = $\frac{-1}{2}$
But we have $\cos \left(\frac{2 \pi}{3}\right)$ = $\cos \left(\pi-\frac{\pi}{3}\right)$ = $-\cos \left(\frac{\pi}{3}\right)$ = $\frac{-1}{2}$
$\therefore$ A = $\frac{2 \pi}{3}$

Q5. There exists a triangle ABC satisfying the conditions
    (A) b sin A < a, A > $\frac { \pi }{ 2 } $
    (B) b sin A = a, A < $\frac { \pi }{ 2 } $   <-- CORRECT
    (C) b sin A = a, a > b
    (D) b sin A < a, a = b, $A = \frac \pi 2$
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Using rule we have $\frac{a}{\sin A}=\frac{b}{\sin B}$ = k
When k = 1 we have 
b sin A = a sin B
When B = $\frac{\pi}{2}$, we get b sin A = a
But we have in a triangle the sum of the three angles is 180 degrees, so if B = 90 degrees then A < $\frac { \pi }{ 2 } $.

Q6. In a $\Delta ABC,$ (b +c) cos A + (c + a) cos B + (a + b) cos C is equal to
    (A) R r
    (B) 0
    (C) a + b + c   <-- CORRECT
    (D) a - b - c
    Answer: C
    Explanation: $(b+c)cosA+(c+a)cosB+(a+b)cosC\\ =b.cosA+c.cosA+c.cosB+a.cosB+a.cosC+b.cosC\\ =\left( b.cosC+c.cosB \right) +\left( a.cosC+c.cosA \right) +\left( b.cosA+a.cosB \right) \\ =a+b+c\quad \quad \quad \quad \left[ using\quad projection\quad rule \right] $

Q7. If the angles of a triangle ABC are in A.P., then
    (A) c^2 = a^2 + b^2 + ab
    (B) a^2 + c^2 - ac = b^2   <-- CORRECT
    (C) c^2 = a^2 + b^2 - ab
    (D) c^2 = a^2 + b^2
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Given angles of a triangle ABC are in A.P$\Rightarrow \frac { A+C }{ 2 } $ = B $\Rightarrow$ A + C = 2B .....(i)
But we have in a triangle A + B + C = 180^o $\Rightarrow$ 3B = 180^o $\Rightarrow$ B = 60^o [using(i)]
Using Cosine Rule we have
b^2 = a^2 + c^2 - 2ac cos B
$\Rightarrow$ b^2 = a^2 + c^2 - 2ac cos 60^o 
$\Rightarrow$ b^2 = a^2 + c^2 - ac [$\because$ cos 60^o = $\frac{1}{2}$]

Q8. The perimeter of a triangle ABC is 6 times the arithmetic mean of the sines of its angles. If the side b is 2, then the angle B is
    (A) 60^o
    (B) 120^o
    (C) 90^o   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 30^o
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Given a + b + c = $\frac{6(\sin A+\sin B+\sin C)}{3}$,
$\Rightarrow$ a + b + c = 2(sin A + sin B + sin C)
Now using sine rule a = k sin A, b = k sin B, c = k sin C
$\Rightarrow$ k (sin A + sin B + sin C) = 2 (sin A + sin B + sin C)
$\Rightarrow$ k = 2
Now when b = 2 we get
sin B = $\frac{b}{k}=\frac{2}{2}$ = 1
$\Rightarrow$ B = 90^o

Q9. In a triangle ABC, $\tan \frac{A+B}{2} \cot \frac{A-B}{2}$ is equal to
    (A) $\frac {a + b} {a - b}$   <-- CORRECT
    (B) $\frac {a - b} {c}$
    (C) $\frac {a - b} {a + b}$
    (D) $\frac {a + b} {c}$
    Answer: A
    Explanation: We have ${ tan\left( \frac { A-B }{ 2 } \right) }=\frac { a-b }{ a+b } .cot\frac { C }{ 2 } $
But in a triangle ABC, we have A + B + C = ${ 180 }^{ \circ }\Rightarrow \frac { C }{ 2 } ={ 90 }^{ \circ }-\left( \frac { A+B }{ 2 } \right) $
$\therefore \tan \left(\frac{A-B}{2}\right)=\frac{a-b}{a+b} \cdot \cot \left[90^{\circ}-\left(\frac{A+B}{2}\right)\right]$
$\Rightarrow \tan \left(\frac{\dot{A}-B}{2}\right)=\frac{a-b}{a+b} \cdot \tan \left(\frac{A+B}{2}\right)$
$\Rightarrow \tan \left(\frac{A+B}{2}\right) \cdot \cot \left(\frac{A-B}{2}\right)=\frac{a+b}{a-b}$

Q10. The area of a triangle is 80 cm^2 and its perimeter is 8 cm. The radius of its inscribed circle is
    (A) 5 cm
    (B) 10 cm
    (C) 20 cm   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 30 cm
    Answer: C
    Explanation: We have if a,b,c are the sides of the triangle and r is the radius of the circle inscribed, then 
r = $\frac{\Delta}{s}$, where 2s = a + b + c and $\Delta$ denotes the area of the triangle.
Given $\Delta$ = 80 and a + b + c = 8
$\therefore$ r = $\frac{\Delta}{s}=\frac{80}{4}$ = 20 cm

Q11. If the angles of a triangle be in the ratio 1 : 4 : 5, then the ratio of the greatest side to the smallest side is
    (A) $4:\left( {\sqrt 5 - 1} \right)$   <-- CORRECT
    (B) $(\sqrt 5 - 1):1$
    (C) 5 : 1
    (D) $1:(\sqrt 5 - 1)$
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Let angles x, 4x and 5x, then x + 4x + 5x = 180^o $\Rightarrow$ 10x = 180 $\Rightarrow$ x = $\frac{180}{10}$ = 18^o 
Using sine formula we have $\frac{a}{\sin A}=\frac{b}{\sin B}=\frac{c}{\sin C}$ = k(say)
Here a = k sin 18, b = k sin 72, c = k sin 90
$\Rightarrow$ a = $\frac{\sqrt{5}-1}{4} \cdot k$, b = $\frac{\sqrt{10+2 \sqrt{5}}}{4} \cdot k$, c = 1(k)
c: a = $1: \frac{\sqrt{5}-1}{4}$ $\Rightarrow$ c: a = 4 : $\sqrt{5}-1$

Q12. If a = 4, b = 3 and A =${60^ \circ }$, then c is a root of the equation
    (A) ${x^2} - 3x - 7 = 0$   <-- CORRECT
    (B) ${x^2} - 2x + 7 = 0$
    (C) ${x^2} - 3x + 8 = 0$
    (D) ${x^2} - 5x + 7 = 0$
    Answer: A
    Explanation: $cosA=\frac { { b }^{ 2 }+{ c }^{ 2 }-a^{ 2 } }{ 2bc } \\ \Rightarrow cos{ 60 }^{ \circ }=\frac { 1 }{ 2 } =\frac { 9+{ c }^{ 2 }-16 }{ 6c } \\ \Rightarrow { c }^{ 2 }-7=3c\\ \Rightarrow { c }^{ 2 }-3c-7=0\\ $
Hence c is the root of the equation ${x^2} - 3x - 7 = 0$

Q13. If b^2 + c^2 = 3a^2, then cot B + cot C - cot A =
    (A) $\frac {ab} {4\Delta }$
    (B) 1
    (C) 0   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $\frac {ac} {4\Delta }$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: We have cos A = $\frac{b^{2}+c^{2}-a^{2}}{2 b c}$, cos B = $\frac{c^{2}+a^{2}-b^{2}}{2 c a}$, cos C = $\frac{a^{2}+b^{2}-c^{2}}{2 a b}$ 
Also sin A = $\frac{2 \Delta}{b c}$, sin B = $\frac{2 \Delta}{c a}$, sin C = $\frac{2 \Delta}{a b}$, where $\Delta$ = $\sqrt{s(s-a)(s-b)(s-c)}$ 
Hence we get cos B + cos C - cot A = $\frac{c^{2}+a^{2}-b^{2}}{4 \Delta}+\frac{a^{2}+b^{2}-c^{2}}{4 \Delta}-\frac{b^{2}+c^{2}-a^{2}}{4 \Delta}$ = $\frac{3 a^{2}-\left(b^{2}+c^{2}\right)}{4 \Delta}=\frac{0}{4 \Delta}$ = 0 [$\because$ 3a^2 = b^2+ c^2]

Q14. $bc{\cos ^2}{\frac A 2} + ca{\cos ^2}{\frac B 2} + ab{\cos ^2}{\frac C 2}$ is equal to
    (A) ${(s - c)^2}$
    (B) ${(s - a)^2}$
    (C) ${s^2}$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) ${(s - b)^2}$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: $b \cos ^{2}\left(\frac{A}{2}\right)+{cacos}^{2}\left(\frac{B}{2}\right)+a b \cos ^{2}\left(\frac{C}{2}\right)$
$=b c\left(\frac{1+\cos A}{2}\right)+c a\left(\frac{1+\cos B}{2}\right)+a b\left(\frac{1+\cos C}{2}\right)$
$=b c\left(\frac{1+\left(\frac{b^{2}+c^{2}-a^{2}}{2 b c}\right)}{2}\right)+c a\left(\frac{1+\left(\frac{c^{2}+a^{2}-b^{2}}{2 c a}\right)}{2}\right)+a b\left(\frac{1+\left(\frac{a^{2}+b^{2}-c^{2}}{2 a b}\right)}{2}\right)$
$=b c\left(\frac{2 b c+b^{2}+c^{2}-a^{2}}{4 b c}\right)+c a\left(\frac{2 c a+c^{2}+a^{2}-b^{2}}{4 c a}\right)+a b\left(\frac{2 a b+a^{2}+b^{2}-c^{2}}{4 a b}\right)$
$=\frac{2 b c+b^{2}+c^{2}-a^{2}+2 c a+c^{2}+a^{2}-b^{2}+2 a b+a^{2}+b^{2}-c^{2}}{4}$
$=\frac{a^{2}+b^{2}+c^{2}+2 b c+2 c a+2 a b}{4}$
$=\left(\frac{a+b+c}{2}\right)^{2} \quad[\because \quad 2 s=a+b+c]$
$=s^{2}$

Q15. If the sides of a triangle be 2x + 1, x^2 - 1 and x^2 + x + 1, then the greatest angle is
    (A) 90^o
    (B) 135^o
    (C) 150^o
    (D) 120^o   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Let the sides of the triangle be a = 2x + 1, b = x^2 - 1 and c = x^2 + x + 1
We have cos C = $\frac{a^{2}+b^{2}-c^{2}}{2 a b}$ = $\frac{(2 x+1)^{2}+\left(x^{2}-1\right)^{2}-\left(x^{2}+x+1\right)^{2}}{2(2 x+1)\left(x^{2}-1\right)}$ 
= $\frac{4 x^{2}+4 x+1+x^{4}-2 x^{2}+1-\left(x^{4}+x^{2}+1+2 x^{3}+2 x^{2}+2 x\right)}{2\left(2 x^{3}-2 x+x^{2}-1\right)}$ 
= $\frac{-2 x^{3}+2 x-x^{2}+1}{2\left(2 x^{3}-2 x+x^{2}-1\right)}$ = $\frac{-1}{2}$ 
Now cos C = $\frac{-1}{2}$ 
But we have $\cos \left(\frac{2 \pi}{3}\right)$ = $\cos \left(\pi-\frac{\pi}{3}\right)$ = $-\cos \left(\frac{\pi}{3}\right)$ = $\frac{-1}{2}$ 
$\therefore$ C = $\frac{2 \pi}{3}$ = 120^o

Trigonometric Functions Test 02  (id 613) [PAID]
CBSE > Class 11 > Mathematics > Trigonometric Functions
Questions: 15 | Answers: YES

Q1. If in a triangle ABC, $a{\cos ^2}\left( {{\frac C2}} \right) + c{\cos ^2}\left( {{\frac A2}} \right) = {\frac {3b} {2}}$ then its sides a, b, c are in
    (A) A. P.   <-- CORRECT
    (B) G.P.
    (C) H.P.
    (D) H.M
    Answer: A
    Explanation: $a \cos ^{2}\left(\frac{C}{2}\right)+c \cos ^{2}\left(\frac{A}{2}\right)$ = $\frac{3 b}{2}$ 
$\Rightarrow$ $a\left(\frac{1+\cos C}{2}\right)+c\left(\frac{1+\cos A}{2}\right)$ = $\frac{3b}{2}$ 
$\Rightarrow$ $\frac{1}{2}$ (b + a + c) = $\frac{3b}{2}$ [$\because$ a cos C + c cos A = b using projection rule]
$\Rightarrow$ a + c = 2b
Which means they are in A.P.

Q2. In a $\Delta ABC$, if a sin A = b sin B then the triangle is
    (A) right angled
    (B) isosceles   <-- CORRECT
    (C) equilateral
    (D) right angled isosceles
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Given a sin A = b sin B .....(i)
But using sine rule we have $\frac{a}{\sin A}=\frac{b}{\sin B}=\frac{c}{\sin C}$ = k
$\therefore$ sin A = $\frac{a}{k}$ and sin B = $\frac{b}{k}$ 
Now from (i) we get $\frac{1}{k} $(a^2 - b^2) = 0 $\Rightarrow$ (a + b) (a - b) = 0 $\Rightarrow$ a = b
Hence the triangle is isosceles.

Q3. If in a $\Delta ABC$ , a cos A = b cos B, then the triangle is
    (A) either isosceles or right angled   <-- CORRECT
    (B) right angled
    (C) equilateral
    (D) isosceles
    Answer: A
    Explanation: a.cos A=b.cos B
$\Rightarrow a \cdot\left(\frac{b^{2}+c^{2}-a^{2}}{2 b c}\right)=b \cdot\left(\frac{c^{2}+a^{2}-b^{2}}{2 c a}\right)$
$\Rightarrow \frac{a^{2}\left(b^{2}+c^{2}-a^{2}\right)-b^{2}\left(c^{2}+a^{2}-b^{2}\right)}{2 a b c}=0$
$\Rightarrow a^{2} c^{2}-b^{2} c^{2}=a^{4}-b^{4}$
$\Rightarrow c^{2}\left(a^{2}-b^{2}\right)=\left(a^{2}-b^{2}\right)\left(a^{2}+b^{2}\right)$
$\Rightarrow a^{2}-b^{2}=0 \quad \text { or } \quad a^{2}+b^{2}=c^{2}$$\Rightarrow a=b \quad \text { or } \quad a^{2}+b^{2}=c^{2}$
Hence the triangle is either isosceles or right angle

Q4. In a $\Delta ABC$, $\frac{\cos ^2\left(\frac{A}{2}\right)}{a}+\frac{\cos ^2\left(\frac{B}{2}\right)}{b}+\frac{\cos ^2\left(\frac{C}{2}\right)}{c}$ is equal to
    (A) $\frac {2s} {abc}$
    (B) $\frac {3s} {abc}$
    (C) $\frac {s} {abc}$
    (D) ${\frac {s^2} {abc}}$   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: $\frac{\cos ^{2}\left(\frac{A}{2}\right)}{a}+\frac{\cos ^{2}\left(\frac{B}{2}\right)}{b}+\frac{\cos ^{2}\left(\frac{c}{2}\right)}{c}$ = $\frac{\frac{1+\cos A}{2}}{a}+\frac{\frac{1+\cos B}{2}}{b}+\frac{\frac{1+\cos C}{2}}{c}$ 
= $\frac { \frac { 1+\left( \frac { { b }^{ 2 }+{ c }^{ 2 }-a^{ 2 } }{ 2bc } \right) }{ 2 } }{ a } +\frac { \frac { 1+\left( \frac { { c }^{ 2 }+a^{ 2 }-{ b }^{ 2 } }{ 2ca } \right) }{ 2 } }{ b } +\frac { \frac { 1+\left( \frac { { a^{ 2 }+b }^{ 2 }-{ c }^{ 2 } }{ 2ab } \right) }{ 2 } }{ c }$ 
= $\frac{2 b c+b^{2}+c^{2}-a^{2}+2 c a+c^{2}+a^{2}-b^{2}+2 a b+a^{2}+b^{2}-c^{2}}{4 a b c}$ 
= $\frac{a^{2}+b^{2}+c^{2}+2 b c+2 c a+2 a b}{4 a b c}$ 
= $\frac{\left(\frac{a+b+c}{2}\right)^{2}}{a b c}$ [$\because$ 2s = a + b + c]
= $\frac{s^{2}}{a b c}$

Q5. In a triangle ABC, a = 2b and $\angle A = 3\angle B,$ then angle A is
    (A) 30^o
    (B) 60^o
    (C) 45^o
    (D) 90^o   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Using Sine rule we have $\frac{a}{\sin A}=\frac{b}{\sin B}=\frac{c}{\sin C}$ = k
But given a = 2b and $\angle A=3 \angle B$ 
$\therefore$ $\frac{a}{\sin A}=\frac{b}{\sin B}$ $\Rightarrow$ $\frac{2 b}{\sin 3 B}=\frac{b}{\sin B}$ 
$\Rightarrow$ sin 3B = 2 sin B
$\Rightarrow$ 3 sin B - 4 sin^3 B = 2 sin B
$\Rightarrow$ sin B (1 - 4 sin^2 B) = 0
$\Rightarrow$ sin^2 B = $\frac{1}{4}$ [$\because$ sin B $\ne$ 0]
$\Rightarrow$ sin B = $\frac{\pm 1}{2}$ 
Now sin B = $\frac{1}{2}$ $\Rightarrow$ B = 30^o and sin B = $\frac{-1}{2}$ $\Rightarrow$ B = 210^o, which is not possible.
$\Rightarrow$ B = 30^o
Hence $\angle A=3 \angle B$ = 90^o

Q6. In a triangle ABC, AD is the median A to BC, then its length is equal to
    (A) ${\frac 12}\sqrt {2({b^2} + {c^2}) - {a^2}} $   <-- CORRECT
    (B) $\sqrt {{b^2} + {c^2} - \frac {a^2} {2}} $
    (C) $\sqrt {{b^2} + {c^2} + \frac {a^2} {2}} $
    (D) $\frac {b + c} {2}$
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Using the given information let the length of the median be d units, and let BD = DB = m units. Using Apollonius theorem we have the following results.
From triangle ADC, if the angle ADC is $\theta$, then b^2 = m^2 + d^2 - 2mdcos $\theta$. The angle ADB is the supplement of angle ADC and it will be - cos $\theta$. Hence from triangle ADB we have b^2 = m^2 + d^2+ 2mdcos $\theta$ . When we add both the results we have b^2 + c^2 = 2 d^2 + 2 m^2 = 2 d^2 + 2 $(\frac{a}{2})^2$ = 2 d^2 + ( $\frac{a^2}{2} $).
On simplifying we get the result.

Q7. If cos A + cos B = $4{\sin ^2}{\frac C 2}$, then the sides of the triangle ABC are in
    (A) G.P.
    (B) H.M
    (C) A.P.   <-- CORRECT
    (D) H.P.
    Answer: C
    Explanation: cos A + cos B = $4 \sin ^{2} \frac{C}{2}$ 
$\Rightarrow$ $2 \cos \left(\frac{A+B}{2}\right) \cdot \cos \left(\frac{A-B}{2}\right)$ = $4 \sin ^{2} \frac{C}{2}$ [$\because$ $\cos \left(\frac{A+B}{2}\right)$ = $\sin \left(\frac{C}{2}\right)$] 
$\Rightarrow$ $\cos \left(\frac{A-B}{2}\right)$ = $2 \sin \frac{C}{2}$ = $\frac{2 \sin \frac{C}{2} \cos \frac{C}{2}}{\cos \frac{C}{2}}$ 
$\Rightarrow$ $\cos \frac{C}{2} \cdot \cos \left(\frac{A-B}{2}\right)$ = sin C [$\because$$\sin \left(\frac{A+B}{2}\right)$ = $\cos \left(\frac{C}{2}\right)$
$\Rightarrow$ $\frac{\sin A+\sin B}{2}$ = sin C [$\because$ 2sin A cos B = sin (A + b) + sin (A - B)]
$\Rightarrow$ sin A, sin B, sin C are in A.P.
$\Rightarrow$ a, b, c are in A.P. using Sine rule

Q8. ABC is an equilateral triangle of each side a ($>$ 0). The inradius of the triangle is
    (A) $\frac a3$
    (B) $\frac{\sqrt{3} a}{3}$
    (C) $\frac{a}{2 \sqrt{3}}$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $\frac a2$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: If r is the inradius of the circle then we have, r = $\frac{\Delta}{s}$ 
Given ABC is an equilateral triangle, so $\triangle$ = $\sqrt{s(s-a)(s-b)(s-c)}$ $\Rightarrow$ $\triangle$ = $\sqrt{s(s-a)(s-a)(s-a)}$ 
$\therefore$ r = $\frac{\Delta}{s}=\frac{s-a}{s} \sqrt{s(s-a)}$ 
Also, s = $\frac{3 a}{2}$ [$\because$ 2s = a + b + c]
$\Rightarrow$ r = $\frac{\frac{3 a}{2}-a}{\frac{3 a}{2}} \sqrt{\frac{3 a}{2}\left(\frac{3 a}{2}-a\right)}$ 
$\Rightarrow$ r = $\frac{a}{2} \cdot \frac{2}{3 a} \sqrt{\frac{3 a^{2}}{4}}=\frac{a}{2 \sqrt{3}}$

Q9. In any $\Delta ABC,$ the expression (a + b + c) (a + b – c) (b + c – a) (c + a – b) is equal to
    (A) $16\Delta $
    (B) $4{\Delta ^2}$   <-- CORRECT
    (C) $3{\Delta ^2}$
    (D) $4\Delta $
    Answer: B
    Explanation: In a triangle A B C, we have 2s=a+b+c
$\therefore a+b-c=2(s-c), b+c-a=2(s-a), c+a-b=2(s-b)$
Hence $(a+b+c)(a+b-c)(b+c-a)(c+a-b)$ 
= $2 s .2(s-c) \cdot 2(s-a) \cdot 2(s-b)$
= $16 s(s-a)(s-b)(s-c)$
But we have $\Delta=\sqrt{s(s-a)(s-b)(s-c)}$
$\therefore(a+b+c)(a+b-c)(b+c-a)(c+a-b)=4 \Delta^{2}$

Q10. In any $\Delta ABC$, if C = 90^o, then tan $\frac B2$ is equal to
    (A) $\sqrt{\frac{c+a}{c-a}}$
    (B) $\sqrt{\frac{a-c}{a+c}}$
    (C) $\sqrt{\frac{c-a}{c+a}}$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $\sqrt{\frac{c-a}{c-a}}$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: In any triangle ABC, A + C = 90^o, since C = 90^o $\Rightarrow$ B = 90^o - A 
We know that $tan(\frac{(C-A)}{2})= \frac{(c-a)}{(c +a)} cot⁡(\frac{B}{2}) $ $\Rightarrow$ $tan (\frac{(90 - A)}{2})$ = $\frac{(c-a)}{(c +a)} (\frac{1}{tan⁡(\frac{B}{2})}) $ 
$\Rightarrow$ $tan (\frac{B}{2}) tan (\frac{B}{2})$ = $\frac{(c-a)}{(c+a)}$ 
$\Rightarrow$ $tan^2 (\frac{B}{2}) $ = $\frac{(c-a)}{(c+a)}$ 
Hence the result follows.

Q11. Angles of triangle are in A.P. If the number of degrees in the smallest to the number of radians in the largest is as $60:\pi $, then the smallest angle is
    (A) ${30^ \circ }$   <-- CORRECT
    (B) none of these.
    (C) ${40^ \circ }$
    (D) ${20^ \circ }$
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Let the measures of the angles of the triangle be a-d, a, a+d degrees respectively
Then we have $a-d+a+a+d=180^{\circ}$
$\Rightarrow 3 a=180^{\circ}$
$\Rightarrow a=\frac{180^{\circ}}{3}=60^{\circ}$
But it is clear that $a-d=60^{\circ}-d$ is the least and $a+d= 60^{\circ}+d$ is the greatest angle.
Given greatest angle = $\pi$ $\text {radian}=180^{\circ}$
$\frac{\text {least angle}}{\text {greatest angle}}=\frac{60^{\circ}}{\pi \text {radian}}$
$\Rightarrow \frac{60^{\circ}-d}{60^{\circ}+d}=\frac{60^{\circ}}{180^{\circ}}=\frac{1}{3}$
$\Rightarrow 180^{\circ}-3 d=60^{\circ}+d$
$\Rightarrow 4 d=120^{\circ}$
$\Rightarrow d=30^{\circ}$

Q12. If x, y, z are perpendiculars drawn from the vertices of a triangle having sides a, b and c, then $\frac{b x}{c}+\frac{c y}{a}+\frac{a z}{b}$ =
    (A) $\frac{a^2+b^2+c^2}{2 R}$   <-- CORRECT
    (B) $\frac{2\left(a^2+b^2+c^2\right)}{R}$
    (C) $\frac{a^2+b^2+c^2}{R}$
    (D) $\frac{a^2+b^2+c^2}{4 R}$
    Answer: A
    Explanation: In triangle ABC given that the length of the altitude from A to BC is x units. let AD be the altitude. from triangle ADC sin C = x/b and from triangle ABD sin B = x/c Hence we get two values of x as x = c sin B and x = b sin C . similarly we have y = a sin C and z = bsin A. so when we substitute in the given equation we get b sin B $\neq$ c sinC $\neq$ a sin A.
The sine formula gives that $\frac{a}{sin⁡A} = \frac{b}{sin⁡B} = \frac{c}{sin⁡C} =2R$ where R is the circumcenter of the triangle.By replacing Sin A = $\frac{a}{2R}$, sin B = $\frac{b}{2R}$, sin C =$\frac{c}{2R}$.
If we use these in b sin B $\neq$ c sinC $\neq$ a sin A. we get the required result.

Q13. The greatest value of sin x cos x is
    (A) $\frac {1}{\sqrt 2 }$
    (B) $\frac {\sqrt 3 } {2}$
    (C) 1
    (D) $\frac 1 2$   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: sin x cos x = $\frac{1}{2} \cdot$2sin x cos x = $\frac{1}{2} \cdot$ sin 2x
But the maximum value of sin 2x is 1.
So the maximum value of sin x cos x = $\frac{1}{2}$

Q14. If $tan\theta + \sec \theta = \sqrt 3 ,0 < \theta< \;\pi ,$ then $\theta $ is equal to
    (A) $\frac {2\pi } {3}$
    (B) $\frac {5\pi }{6}$
    (C) $\frac \pi 3$
    (D) $\frac \pi 6$   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: $tan\theta +\sec \theta =\sqrt { 3 } $
$\Rightarrow { sec }^{ 2 }x={ \left[ \sqrt { 3 } -tan\theta \right] }^{ 2 }$
$\Rightarrow \left( 1+{ tan }^{ 2 }x \right) =3+{ tan }^{ 2 }x-2\sqrt { 3 } tanx$
$\Rightarrow 2\sqrt { 3 } tanx=2$
$tanx=\frac { 1 }{ \sqrt { 3 } } $
$\Rightarrow tan x=tan\left( \frac { \pi }{ 6 } \right)$
$\Rightarrow x=n\pi +\left( \frac { \pi }{ 6 } \right) ,n \in Z$
$\Rightarrow x=\frac { \pi }{ 6 } ,0<\theta < \pi $

Q15. If sin $({120^o} - \alpha ) = \sin ({120^o} - \beta )$ and 0 $ < \alpha ,\beta {\rm{ }} < {\rm{ }}\pi $ , then all values of $ \alpha$ and $\beta$ are given by
    (A) $\alpha = \beta \;$
    (B) $\alpha + \beta \; = 0$
    (C) $\alpha+\beta=\frac{\pi}{3}$
    (D) $\alpha = \beta $ or $\alpha+\beta=\frac{\pi}{3}$   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Sin A = sin B will be sin A - sin B = 0 using the formula we get,
2 cos $\frac{A+B}{2}$ sin $\frac{A-B}{2}$ = 0
Replacing A by 120 - $α$ and B by 120 - $β$ we get 
$cos(\frac{(120- \alpha+120- \beta)}{2})$ = 0
$cos⁡[\frac{(240- \alpha - \beta)}{2}] $ = 0 
$\Rightarrow$ 120 - $\alpha$ - $\beta$ = $\pi$ 
$\Rightarrow$ $\alpha + \beta =60= \frac{\pi}{3}$ the other result.
Replacing A by 120 - $α$ and B by 120 - $β$ we get sin A- B = 0 which gives $α=β$

Q1. The equation (cos p – 1) ${x^2}$ + (cos p) x + sin p = 0, where x is a variable, has real roots. Then the interval of p may be any one of the following:38100
    (A) $\left(-\frac{\pi}{2}, \frac{\pi}{2}\right)$
    (B) $\left( {1,\pi } \right)$
    (C) $\left( { - \pi ,0} \right)$
    (D) $\left( {0,\pi } \right)$   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: $(cosp–1){ x }^{ 2 }+(cosp)x+sinp=0\\ We\quad have\quad the\quad roots\quad are\quad real\quad when\quad the\quad discriminant\ge 0\\ \Longrightarrow { cos }^{ 2 }p-4(cosp–1).sinp\ge 0\\ \Longrightarrow { cos }^{ 2 }p-4cospsinp+4sinp\ge 0\\ \Longrightarrow { (cosp–2sinp) }^{ 2 }-4{ sin }^{ 2 }p+4sinp\ge 0\\ \Longrightarrow { (cosp–2sinp) }^{ 2 }+4sinp\left( 1-sinp \right) \ge 0...........(i)$
We have for all values of p 
Hence from equation (i) we get $sin \:p$ has to be non-negative for all the values of p
But we have $sin \: p \ge0$ only when $ p\in \left( 0,\pi \right) $

Q2. In a triangle ABC, a = 13, b = 14, c = 15; then the radius of the incircle r of triangle ABC =
    (A) 2
    (B) 8
    (C) 6
    (D) 4   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: If r is the inradius of the circle then we have, r = $\frac{\Delta}{s}$ 
Given a = 13, b = 14 and c = 15 and so s = $\frac{a+b+c}{2}$ = $\frac{ 42}{2}$ = 21
$\therefore$ $\Delta$ = $\sqrt{s(s-a)(s-b)(s-c)}$ = $\sqrt{21(21-13)(21-14)(21-15)}$ = 84
r = $\frac{\Delta}{s}$ = $\frac{84}{21}$ = 4

Q3. If sin $\theta + {\rm{ cosec }}\theta $ = 2, then ${\sin ^2}\theta + \cos e{c^2}\theta $ is equal to
    (A) 4
    (B) 2   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 3
    (D) 1
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Given $\sin \theta+cosec \theta=2$ 
Squaring on both sides, we get 
$\sin ^{2} \theta+\csc ^{2} \theta+2 \sin \theta \csc \theta=4$ [$\because$ $\sin \theta cosec \theta=1$]
$\Rightarrow$ $\sin ^{2} \theta+cosec ^{2} \theta$ = 4 - 2 = 2

Q4. The value of tan ${75^ \circ }$ - cot ${75^ \circ }$ is equal to
    (A) $2\sqrt 3 $   <-- CORRECT
    (B) $1 + 2\sqrt 3 $
    (C) $2 - \sqrt 3 $
    (D) $2 + \sqrt 3 $
    Answer: A
    Explanation: tan 75° = tan(45° + 30°
$= \frac{{1 + \frac{1}{{\sqrt 3 }}}}{{1 - \frac{1}{{\sqrt 3 }}}} = \frac{{\sqrt 3 + 1}}{{\sqrt 3 - 1}}\therefore cot{75^\circ } = \frac{{\sqrt 3 - 1}}{{\sqrt 3 + 1}}$
Now $tan{75^\circ } - cot{75^\circ } = \frac{{\sqrt 3 + 1}}{{\sqrt 3 - 1}} - \frac{{\sqrt 3 - 1}}{{\sqrt 3 + 1}}$
$ = \frac{{{{\left( {\sqrt 3 + 1} \right)}^2} - {{\left( {\sqrt 3 - 1} \right)}^2}}}{{\left( {\sqrt 3 + 1} \right)\left( {\sqrt 3 - 1} \right)}}$
$\Rightarrow tan{75^\circ } - cot{75^\circ } = \frac{{4\sqrt 3 }}{2} = 2\sqrt 3 $

Q5. In a triangle ABC, cosec A (sin B cos C + cos B sin C) equals
    (A) 2
    (B) -1
    (C) 1   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $\frac ac$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: cosec A (sin B cos C + cos B sin C)
= cosec A$\cdot$sin (B + C) [$\because$ A + B + C = $\pi$]
= $\frac{\sin (B+C)}{\sin A}=\frac{\sin (\pi-A)}{\sin A}=\frac{\sin A}{\sin A}$ = 1

Q6. If the angles of at triangle are in the ratio 1 : 2 : 3, then the sides are in the ratio
    (A) $\sqrt 3 :\sqrt 2 :1$
    (B) $\sqrt 3 :1:2$
    (C) $1:\sqrt 3 :2$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $1:\sqrt 3 :\sqrt {2} $
    Answer: C
    Explanation: $Let\; angles\; x,2x\; and\; 3x\quad then,x+2x+3x={ 180^{ o } }\Rightarrow 6x=180\Rightarrow x{ { =\frac { 180 }{ 6 } } }={ 30^{ o\\ } }\\ Using\; sine\quad rule\quad we\quad have\frac { a }{ sinA } =\frac { b }{ sinB } =\frac { c }{ sinC } =k(say)\\ Herea=k\sin 30,b=k\sin 60,c=k\sin 90\\ \Rightarrow a=\frac { { { k } } }{ 2 } ,b={ { \frac { \sqrt { 3 } }{ 2 } } }.k,c={ 1 }.k\\ a{ { :b{ { : } }c } }=\frac { { { k } } }{ 2 } { { :\frac { k\sqrt { 3 } }{ 2 } { { : } } } }{ 1 }.k\\ \Rightarrow a{ { :b{ { : } }c } }=1{ { :\sqrt { 3 } { { : } }2 } }$

Q7. In a triangle ABC right angled at C, tan A and tan B satisfy the equation
    (A) $a{x^2} - bx + a = 0$
    (B) $ab{x^2} - {c^2}x + ab = 0$   <-- CORRECT
    (C) $ab{x^2} - ({a^2} + {b^2})x - ab = 0$
    (D) ${c^2}{x^2} - abx + {c^2} = 0$
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Using the given triangle we have tan A = a/b and tan B = b/a Also we have tan A . tan B = 1 and tan A + tan B = a/b + b /a = ( a^2 + b^2 )/ab = c^2 / ab . Hence x = tan A and x = tan B are two roots of the given quadratic equation.

Q8. Let the angles A, B, C of $\Delta ABC$ be in A.P. and let b: c:: $\sqrt 3 :\sqrt 2 ,$ then the angle A is
    (A) 90
    (B) 45^o
    (C) 60^o
    (D) 75^o   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Since A, B, C are in A.P. we get $\frac{A+C}{2}$ = B $\Rightarrow$ A + C = 2B .....(i)
But in triangle ABc, we have A + B + C = $\pi$ $\Rightarrow$ 3B = $\pi$ $\Rightarrow$ B = $\frac{\pi}{3}$ 
From (i) A + C = $2 \frac{\pi}{3}$ $\Rightarrow$ C = $\frac{2 \pi}{3}-A$ 
Using sine rule we have $\frac{a}{\sin A}=\frac{b}{\sin B}=\frac{c}{\sin C}$ = k (say)
Now $\frac{b}{\sin B}=\frac{c}{\sin C}$ $\Rightarrow$ $\frac{b}{\sin \frac{\pi}{3}}=\frac{c}{\sin \left(\frac{2 \pi}{3}-A\right)}$ 
$\Rightarrow$ $\sin \left(\frac{2 \pi}{3}-A\right)$ = $\frac{c}{b} \cdot \sin \frac{\pi}{3}=\frac{\sqrt{2}}{\sqrt{3}} \cdot \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}=\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}$ [$\because$ b : c = $\sqrt{3}: \sqrt{2}$ ]
$\Rightarrow$ $\sin \left(\frac{2 \pi}{3}-A\right)$ = $\sin \left(\frac{\pi}{4}\right)$ 
$\Rightarrow$ $\frac{2 \pi}{3}-A$ = $\frac{\pi}{4}$ 
$\Rightarrow$ A = $\frac{5 \pi}{12}$ = 75^o

Q9. cot $\theta $ = sin 2 $\theta (\theta $ $ \ne $n $\pi $, n integer) if $\theta $ equals
    (A) 45^o and 60^o
    (B) 90^o only
    (C) 45^o only
    (D) 45^o and 90^o   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: sin 2$\theta$ = cot $\theta$ 
$\Rightarrow$ 2 sin $\theta$ cos $\theta$ = $\frac{\sin \theta}{\cos \theta}$ 
$\Rightarrow$ $\cos \theta\left(2 \sin \theta-\frac{1}{\sin \theta}\right)$ = 0
$\Rightarrow$ -cos $\theta$ (1 - 2 sin^2 $\theta$) = 0
$\Rightarrow$ -cos $\theta$ $\cdot$ cos 2$\theta$ = 0
$\Rightarrow$ cos $\theta$ = cos $\frac{\pi}{2}$ or cos 2$\theta$ = cos $\frac{\pi}{2}$ 
$\Rightarrow$ $\theta$ = $\frac{\pi}{2}, \frac{\pi}{4}$ [$\because$ $\theta \neq n \pi$, n $\in$ Z]

Q10. If A = ${\sin ^2}\theta + {\cos ^4}\theta $ then for all real values of $\theta $
    (A) ${\frac 3 4} \le A \le 1$   <-- CORRECT
    (B) $\frac{3}{4} \leq A \leq \frac{13}{16}$
    (C) $1 \le A \le 2$
    (D) $\frac {13} {16} \le A \le 1$
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Given, A = $\sin ^{2} \theta+\cos ^{4} \theta$ 
$\Rightarrow$ A = $\sin ^{2} \theta+\left(1-\sin ^{2} \theta\right)^{2}$ 
$\Rightarrow$ A = $\sin ^{4} \theta-\sin ^{2} \theta+1$ 
Let $\sin ^{2} \theta$ = x, then
We get A = $x^{2}-x+\frac{1}{4}+\frac{3}{4}$ 
$\Rightarrow$ A = $\left(x-\frac{1}{2}\right)^{2}+\frac{3}{4}$ 
But we have $\sin ^{2} \theta$ = x ranges in the interval [0, 1]
Hence we get $\frac{3}{4} \leq A \leq 1$

Q11. The general solution of tan 3x = 1 is (n $ \in $ I)
    (A) $n\pi \pm {\frac \pi 4}$
    (B) ${\rm{n}}\pi $
    (C) $\frac{n \pi}{3}+\frac{\pi}{12}$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $n\pi + {\frac \pi 4}$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: tan 3x = 1
$\Rightarrow$ tan (3x) = tan ($\frac{\pi}{4}$)
$\Rightarrow$ 3x = $n \pi+\frac{\pi}{4}, n \in Z$ 
$\Rightarrow$ x = $\frac{n \pi}{3}+\frac{\pi}{12}, n \in Z$

Q12. The value of $\sin \frac{\pi}{14} \sin \frac{3 \pi}{14} \sin \frac{5 \pi}{14} \sin$$ \frac{7 \pi}{14} \sin \frac{9 \pi}{14} \sin \frac{11 \pi}{14} \sin \frac{12}{1}$ is
    (A) $\frac {1}{16}$
    (B) $\frac {1}{32}$
    (C) $\frac {1}{128}$
    (D) $\frac {1}{64}$   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: First, convert the odd multiples of $\frac{\pi}{14}$ to even multiples using the formulas $\sin \theta=\cos \left(\frac{\pi}{2}-\theta\right)$ and $\cos \theta=\sin \left(\frac{\pi}{2}+\theta\right)$ 
So, we get $\sin \frac{\pi}{14}=\cos \frac{6 \pi}{14}$, $\sin \frac{3 \pi}{14}=\cos \frac{4 \pi}{14}$, $\sin \frac{5 \pi}{14}=\cos \frac{2 \pi}{14}$, $\sin \frac{7 \pi}{14}$ = 1
$\sin \frac{9 \pi}{14}=\sin \left(\frac{\pi}{2}+\frac{2 \pi}{14}\right)$ = $\cos \frac{2 \pi}{14}$, $\sin \frac{11 \pi}{14}=\sin \left(\frac{\pi}{2}+\frac{4 \pi}{14}\right)$ = $\cos \frac{4 \pi}{14}, \sin \frac{13 \pi}{14}$ = $\sin \left(\frac{\pi}{2}+\frac{6 \pi}{14}\right)=\cos \frac{6 \pi}{14}$ 
Now $\sin \frac{\pi}{14} \sin \frac{3 \pi}{14} \sin \frac{5 \pi}{14} \sin \frac{7 \pi}{14} \sin \frac{9 \pi}{14} \sin \frac{11 \pi}{14} \sin \frac{13 \pi}{14}$ 
= $\cos ^{2} \frac{6 \pi}{14} \cdot \cos ^{2} \frac{4 \pi}{14} \cdot \cos ^{2} \frac{2 \pi}{14}$ 
= $\frac{\left(\sin ^{2} \frac{2 \pi}{14} \cdot \cos ^{2} \frac{2 \pi}{14}\right) \cdot \cos ^{2} \frac{4 \pi}{14} \cdot \cos ^{2} \frac{6 \pi}{14}}{\sin ^{2} \frac{2 \pi}{14}}$ [$\because$ sin 2x = 2 sinx cos x]
= $\frac{\frac{1}{4} \sin ^{2} \frac{4 \pi}{14} \cdot \cos ^{2} \frac{4 \pi}{14} \cdot \cos ^{2} \frac{6 \pi}{14}}{\sin ^{2} \frac{2 \pi}{14}}$ 
= $\frac{\frac{1}{4} \cdot \frac{1}{4} \cdot \sin ^{2} \frac{8 x}{14} \cdot \cos ^{2} \frac{6 \pi}{14}}{\sin ^{2} \frac{2 \pi}{14}}$ [$\because$ sin 2x = 2 sin x cos x]
= $\frac{\frac{1}{16} \cdot\left(\sin \frac{8 \pi}{14} \cdot \cos \frac{6 \pi}{14}\right)^{2}}{\sin ^{2} \frac{2 \pi}{14}}$ 
= $\frac{\frac{1}{16} \cdot\left(\frac{1}{2}\right)^{2} \cdot\left(\sin \frac{14 \pi}{14}+\sin \frac{2 \pi}{14}\right)^{2}}{\sin ^{2} \frac{2 \pi}{14}}$ [$\because$ 2 sin A cos B = sin (A + B) + sin (A - B)]
= $\frac{\frac{1}{64} \cdot\left(0+\sin \frac{2 \pi}{14}\right)^{2}}{\sin ^{2} \frac{2 \pi}{14}}$ [$\because$ sin $\pi$ = 0]
= $\frac{1}{64}$

Q13. If the radius of the circumcircle of an isosceles triangle PQR is equal to PQ ( = PR), then the angle P is
    (A) $\frac {2\pi } {3}$   <-- CORRECT
    (B) $\frac \pi 6$
    (C) $\frac {\pi}{3}$
    (D) $\frac \pi 2$
    Answer: A
    Explanation: We have if R is the radius of the circumcircle of triangle PQR then we have
$R=\frac{P Q}{2 \sin R}=\frac{Q R}{2 \sin P}=\frac{P R}{2 \sin Q}$ ...(i)
But given radius of the circumcircle R = PQ = PR ...(ii)
From (i) and (ii), we get $\frac{1}{2 \sin R}=1 $ and $\frac{1}{2 \sin Q}=1$
$\Rightarrow \sin R=\sin Q=\frac{1}{2}$
$\Rightarrow \angle R=\angle Q=30^{\circ}$
But in a triangle $\angle P+\angle Q+\angle R=180^{\circ}$
So, $\angle P=180^{\circ}-30^{\circ}-30^{\circ}=120^{\circ}=\frac{2 \pi}{3}$

Q14. The maximum value of sin $\left(x+\frac{\pi}{6}\right)+\cos \left(x+\frac{\pi}{6}\right)$ in the interval $\left(0, \frac{\pi}{2}\right)$ is attained at
    (A) $\frac {\pi}{12}$   <-- CORRECT
    (B) $\frac \pi 3$
    (C) $\frac \pi 2$
    (D) $\frac \pi 6$
    Answer: A
    Explanation: $\sin \left(x+\frac{\pi}{6}\right)+\cos \left(x+\frac{\pi}{6}\right)$
$\sin x \cdot \cos \frac{\pi}{6}+\cos x \cdot \sin \frac{\pi}{6}$$+\cos x \cdot \cos \frac{\pi}{6}-\sin x \cdot \sin \frac{\pi}{6}$
$=\sin x \cdot \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}+\cos x \cdot \frac{1}{2}+\cos x \cdot \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}-\sin x \cdot \frac{1}{2}$
$=\left(\frac{\sqrt{3}-1}{2}\right) \sin x+\left(\frac{\sqrt{3}+1}{2}\right) \cos x$
$=\sqrt{2}\left[\sin 15^{\circ} \sin x+\cos 15^{\circ} \cos x\right]$
$=\sqrt{2}\left[\cos \left(x-15^{\circ}\right)\right]$
But we have the maximum value of cos x is 1, when x = 0
Hence $\cos \left(x-15^{\circ}\right)$ takes its maximum value when $x-15^{\circ}=0 \Longrightarrow$ $x=15^{\circ} \Longrightarrow x=\frac{\pi}{12}$

Q15. The solution of tan 2 $\theta $ tan $\theta $ = 1 is
    (A) $(4n + 1){\frac \pi 6}$
    (B) $(2n \pm 1){\frac \pi 6}$
    (C) $(2n + 1){\frac \pi 3}$
    (D) $(2n+1){ \frac { \pi }{ 6 } }$   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: $\tan 2 \theta \cdot \tan \theta$ = 1
$\Rightarrow$ $\tan 2 \theta=\cot \theta$ 
$\Rightarrow$ $\tan 2 \theta$ = $\tan \left(\frac{\pi}{2}-\theta\right)$ 
$\Rightarrow$ $ 2 \theta$ = $n \pi+\left(\frac{\pi}{2}-\theta\right)$ 
$\Rightarrow$ $ 3 \theta$ = $n \pi+\frac{\pi}{2}$ 
$\Rightarrow$ $ \theta$ = $\frac{n \pi}{3}+\frac{\pi}{6}$ 
$\Rightarrow$ $ \theta$ = $(2 n+1) \frac{\pi}{6}, n \in Z$

Trigonometric Functions Test 04  (id 615) [PAID]

Q1. sin $\left( {180 + \phi } \right)$ sin$\left( {180 - \phi } \right)$ $cose{c^2}\phi $
    (A) - 1   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 1
    (C) 2
    (D) 0
    Answer: A
    Explanation: $\sin (180+\phi)(180-\phi) \operatorname{cosec}^{2} \phi$ = $-\sin \phi \cdot \sin \phi \operatorname{cosec}^{2} \phi$ = $-\sin ^{2} \phi \operatorname{cosec}^{2} \phi$ = -1

Q2. The general solution of the equation sin x + cos x = 2 is
    (A) $3\;n\;\pi + \frac \pi 2$ or 2n$\pi$, n $\in$ I
    (B) $\;n\;\pi ,n \in I$
    (C) $2\;n\;\pi + {\frac \pi 2}$ or 2n$\pi$, n $\in$ I
    (D) no solution   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Given $sinx+cosx=2$ ...(i)
This is of the form acos x + bsin x = c
Now divide the equation (i) throughout by $\sqrt { { a }^{ 2 }+{ b }^{ 2 } } =\sqrt { 1+1 } =\sqrt { 2 } $, we get
$\frac { 1 }{ \sqrt { 2 } } cosx+\frac { 1 }{ \sqrt { 2 } } sinx=\frac { 2 }{ \sqrt { 2 } }$
$\Rightarrow cos\left( \frac { \pi }{ 4 } \right) cosx+sinxsin\left( \frac { \pi }{ 4 } \right) =\sqrt { 2 }$
$\Rightarrow cos\left[ \frac { \pi }{ 4 } -x \right] =\sqrt { 2 } > 1$
So no solution possible

Q3. Which of the following is not possible?
 $\cos \theta=\frac{1+t^2}{1-t^2}, t \neq 0$
 $\sin \theta = \frac 57$
 $\tan \theta = 100$
 $\sec \theta = \frac 52$
    (A) D
    (B) B
    (C) C
    (D) A   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: The value of $\cos \theta$ lies between - 1 and 1.
But when t = 2 the value of $\cos \theta=\frac{1+t^2}{1-t^2}, t \neq 0$ is $\frac { 5 }{ -3 } $ which is more than 1, so it is not possible.

Q4. If (1 + tan $\theta $) (1 + tan $\phi $) = 2, then $\theta $ + $\phi $ =
    (A) 30^o
    (B) 75^o
    (C) 45^o   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 60^o
    Answer: C
    Explanation: $(1+\tan \theta)(1+\tan \phi)$ = 2 $\Rightarrow$ $1+\tan \theta+\tan \phi+\tan \theta \tan \phi$ = 2
$\Rightarrow$ $\tan \theta+\tan \phi+\tan \theta \tan \phi$ = 1
$\Rightarrow$ $\tan \theta+\tan \phi$ = $1-\tan \theta \tan \phi$ 
$\Rightarrow$ $\frac{\tan \theta+\tan \phi}{1-\tan \theta \tan \phi}$ = 1
$\Rightarrow$ $\tan (\theta+\phi)$ = 1
$\Rightarrow$ $\theta+\phi$ = 45^o [tan 45^o = 1]

Q5. The set of values of x for which $\frac{\tan 3 x-\tan 2 x}{1+\tan 3 x \tan 2 x}$ = 1 is
    (A) $\left\{n \pi+\frac{\pi}{4}: n \epsilon Z\right\}$   <-- CORRECT
    (B) $\left\{2 n \pi+\frac{\pi}{4}: n=1,2,3 \ldots\right\}$
    (C) $\left\{\frac{\pi}{4}\right\}$
    (D) $\phi $
    Answer: A
    Explanation: $\frac{\tan 3 x-\tan 2 x}{1+\tan 3 x \tan 2 x}$ = 1 $\Rightarrow$ tan x = 1
$\Rightarrow$ tan (x) = tan ($\frac{\pi}{4}$) [$\because$ tan (A - B) = $\frac{\tan A-\tan B}{1+\tan A \tan B}$]
$\Rightarrow$ x = $n \pi+\frac{\pi}{4}, n \in Z$

Q6. The general value of $\theta $ satisfying the equation $2{\sin ^2}\theta - 3\sin \theta - 2 = 0$ is
    (A) $n\pi + {( - 1)^n}{\frac \pi 2}$
    (B) $n\pi + {( - 1)^n}{\frac {5\pi } {6}}$
    (C) $n\pi + {( - 1)^n}{\frac \pi 6}$
    (D) $n \pi+(-1)^{n+1} \frac{\pi}{6}$   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: $2 \sin ^2 \theta-3 \sin \theta-2=0$
$ \Rightarrow \left( 2\sin \theta +1 \right) \left( \sin \theta -2 \right) =0$
$ \Rightarrow \sin \theta =\frac { -1 }{ 2 } $ or $\sin \theta =2$
$\Rightarrow \sin \theta=\sin \left(-\frac{\pi}{6}\right)$ [$\because$ sin $\theta$ = 2 > 1 so no solution]
$\Rightarrow \theta =n\pi +{ \left( -1 \right) }^{ n }\left( -\frac { \pi }{ 6 } \right) ,n\epsilon Z$
$\Rightarrow \theta =n\pi +{ \left( -1 \right) }^{ n+1 }\left( \frac { \pi }{ 6 } \right) ,n\epsilon Z$

Q7. Number of solutions of the equation tan x + sec x = 2 cos x. lying in the interval $\left[ {0,2\pi } \right]$ is
    (A) 2   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 3
    (C) 0
    (D) 1
    Answer: A
    Explanation: $ \tan x+\sec x=2 \cos x $
$ \frac{\sin x+1}{\cos x}=2 \cos x $
$ 1+\sin x-2 \cos ^2 x=0 $
$ 1+\sin x-2+2 \sin ^2 x=0 $
$ 2 \sin ^2 x+\sin x-1=0 $
Since the equation is a quadratic equation in $\sin x$. So, there will be two solutions.

Q8. In a triangle ABC, if a, b, c are in A. P., then tan $\frac A2,\tan \frac B2, \tan \frac C2$ are in
    (A) A.P.
    (B) H.M.
    (C) G.P.
    (D) H.P.   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: If a, b, c are in A.P then we have $s-a, s-b, s-c$ are also in A . P
We have $\tan \frac{A}{2}=\sqrt{\frac{(s-b)(s-c)}{s(s-a)}}, \tan \frac{B}{2}=\sqrt{\frac{(s-a)(s-c)}{s(s-b)}}, \tan \frac{C}{2}=\sqrt{\frac{(s-a)(s-b)}{s(s-c)}}$
Multipling and dividing$\tan \frac{A}{2} \quad b y(s-a), \tan \frac{B}{2} \quad b y \quad(s-b) \text { and } \quad \tan \frac{C}{2}$ by $(s-b) a n d \quad \tan \frac{C}{2} \quad b y(s-c)$, we get
$\tan \frac{A}{2}=\sqrt{\frac{(s-b)(s-c)}{s(s-a)}}=\frac{\sqrt{\frac{(s-a)(s-b)(s-c)}{s}}}{s-a}=\frac{K}{s-a}$
Similarly we can get $\tan \frac{B}{2}=\frac{K}{s-b}$ and $\tan \frac{C}{2}=\frac{K}{s-c}$, where K=$\sqrt{\frac{(s-a)(s-b)(s-c)}{s}}$
We have ifa, b, c are in A.P then $\frac{1}{a}, \frac{1}{b}, \frac{1}{c}$ are in H.P
$\therefore \quad s-a, s-b, s-c$ are in $A \cdot P \cdot \Rightarrow \frac{K}{s-a}, \frac{K}{s-b}, \frac{K}{s-c}$ are in H.P
$\Rightarrow$ $\tan \frac{A}{2}, \tan \frac{B}{2}, \tan \frac{C}{2}$ are in H.P

Q9. In a $\Delta ABC,$ $\frac{\cos A}{a}=\frac{\cos B}{b}=\frac{\cos C}{c}$ and the side a = 2, then area of the triangle is
    (A) $\frac {\sqrt 3 }{2}$
    (B) 1
    (C) $\sqrt 3 $   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 2
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Given $\frac{\cos A}{a}=\frac{\cos B}{b}=\frac{\cos C}{c}$.....(i)
Using Sine rule we have $\frac{a}{\sin A}=\frac{b}{\sin B}=\frac{c}{\sin C}=k$....(ii)
From(i) and (ii) we get $\frac{\cos A}{\sin A}=\frac{\cos B}{\sin B}=\frac{\cos C}{\sin C}$
$\Rightarrow $ cot A = cot B = cot C
In a triangle this is possible only when $A=B=C=60^{\circ}$ 
Hence the triangle is equilateral
So a = b = c = 2
$\therefore \text { Area }=\frac{1}{2} a b . \sin C=\frac{1}{2} 2.2 . \sin 60^{\circ}=\sqrt{3}$

Q10. In a triangle ABC , a = 4, b = 3 and A = ${60^ \circ }$ ; then c is a root of the equation
    (A) ${c^2} - 3c + 7 = 0$
    (B) ${c^2} - 3c - 8 = 0$
    (C) ${c^2} - 3c - 7 = 0$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) ${c^2} + 3c + 7 = 0$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: $Using\quad Cosine\quad Rule\quad we\quad have\quad cosA=\frac { { b }^{ 2 }+{ c }^{ 2 }-a^{ 2 } }{ 2bc } \\ \Rightarrow cos{ 60 }^{ \circ }=\frac { 1 }{ 2 } =\frac { 9+{ c }^{ 2 }-16 }{ 6c } \\ \Rightarrow { c }^{ 2 }-7=3c\\ \Rightarrow { c }^{ 2 }-3c-7=0\\ \Rightarrow c\quad is\quad the\quad root\quad of\quad the\quad equation\quad { x }^{ 2 }-3x-7=0$

Q11. Which of the following is not correct?
    (A) tan $\theta $ = 20
    (B) $\sin \theta = - {\frac 1 5}$
    (C) $\sec \theta = \frac 1 2$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $\cos \theta = 1$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: $\sec \theta =\frac { 1 }{ 2 } \Rightarrow cos\theta =2$ 
which is not possible since $cos\theta$ is a periodic function whose value oscillates between -1 and 1

Q12. If x = $y \cos \frac{2 \pi}{3}=z \cos \frac{4 \pi}{3}$, then xy + yz + zx =
    (A) 1
    (B) 2
    (C) 0   <-- CORRECT
    (D) -1
    Answer: C
    Explanation: x = $y \cos \frac{2 \pi}{3}$ = $z \cos \frac{4 \pi}{3}$ 
$\Rightarrow$ x = $y \cos \left(\pi-\frac{\pi}{3}\right)$ = $z \cos \left(\pi+\frac{\pi}{3}\right)$ 
$\Rightarrow$ x = $-y \cos \left(\frac{\pi}{3}\right)$ = $-z \cos \left(\frac{\pi}{3}\right)$ 
$\Rightarrow$ x = $-y \times \frac{1}{2}$ = $-z \times \frac{1}{2}$ 
$\Rightarrow$ x = $\frac{-y}{2}=\frac{-z}{2}$ = k (say)
$\Rightarrow$ x = k, y = -2k, z = -2k
$\therefore$ xy + yz + zx = -2k^2 + 4k^2 - 2k^2 = 0

Q13. The values of $\theta $ (0 < $\theta $ < 360^o) satisfying cosec $\theta $ + 2 = 0 are
    (A) 240^o, 300^o
    (B) 210^o, 330^o   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 210^o, 240^o
    (D) 210^o, 300^o
    Answer: B
    Explanation: cosec $\theta$ + 2 = 0 $\Rightarrow$ cosec $\theta$ = -2
$\Rightarrow$ sin $\theta$ = $\frac{-1}{2}$ 
$\Rightarrow$ sin $\theta$ = $\sin \left(-\frac{\pi}{6}\right)$ 
$\Rightarrow$ $\theta$ = $n \pi+(-1)^{n}\left(-\frac{\pi}{6}\right), n \epsilon Z$ 
Hence the values of $\theta$ betwwen 0 and 360 are 210^o, 330^o [when n = 1, n = 2]

Q14. If z_1 and z_2 are non-real complex numbers such that z_1 and z_2 and z_1z_2 are real numbers, then
    (A) ${z_1} = - \overline {{z_2}} $
    (B) ${z_1} = \overline {{z_2}} $   <-- CORRECT
    (C) ${z_1} = \overline {{z_1}} $ and ${z_2} = \overline {{z_2}} $
    (D) ${z_2} = \overline {{z_2}} $
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Let z_1 = x_1 + iy_1 and z_2 = x_2 + iy_2 
Given z_1 and z_2 are non-real complex numbers, so we have ${ y }_{ 1 }\neq 0,{ y }_{ 2 }\neq 0$ 
z_1 + z_2 = (x + iy_1) + (x_2 + iy_2) = (x_1 + x_2) + i(y_1 + y_2) 
z_1 + z_2 is a real number 
$\Rightarrow$ y_1 + y_2 = 0 $\Rightarrow$ y_1 = -y_2 .....(i)
Now z_1z_2 = (x_1 + iy_1) $\cdot$ (x_2 + iy_2) = (x_1x_2 - y_1y_2) + i(x_1y_1 + x_1y_2)
z_1z_2 is a real number $\Rightarrow$ x_2y_1 + x_1y_2 = 0 $\Rightarrow$ x_2y_1 = -x_1y_2 ....(ii)
Now from (i) and (ii) we get $\Rightarrow$ x_2y_1 = x_1y_1 $\Rightarrow$ x_2 = x_1 
Hence we get z_1 = x_2 - iy_2 and z_2 = x_2 + iy_2 
So ${z_1} = \overline {{z_2}} $

Q15. If $sin\alpha = {\rm{ sin}}\beta $ and ${\rm{cos}}\alpha = {\rm{ cos}}\beta $ , then
    (A) $\sin {{(\alpha + \beta }) } = 0$
    (B) $\cos {{(\alpha + \beta) } } = 0$
    (C) $\cos {{(\alpha - \beta) }} = 0$
    (D) $\sin {{(\alpha - \beta )}} = 0$   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Given $\sin \alpha=\sin \beta$ and $\cos \alpha=\cos \beta$ 
Now $\sin \alpha \cdot \cos \beta$ = $\cos \alpha \cdot \sin \beta$ 
$\Rightarrow$ $\sin \alpha \cdot \cos \beta-\cos \alpha \cdot \sin \beta$ = 0
$\Rightarrow$ $\sin (\alpha-\beta)$ = 0

Trigonometric Functions Test 05  (id 616) [PAID]

Q1. If $sin\alpha + {\rm{ sin}}\beta $ = 0 = ${\rm{cos}}\alpha + {\rm{ cos}}\beta $, then ${\rm{cos 2}}\alpha + {\rm{ cos 2}}\beta $ =
    (A) 2 ${\rm{sin }}(\alpha + \beta )$
    (B) - 2 ${\rm{sin }}(\alpha + \beta )$
    (C) - 2 ${\rm{cos }}(\alpha + \beta )$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 2 ${\rm{cos }}(\alpha + \beta )$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: $\cos 2 \alpha+\cos 2 \beta$ 
= $2 \cos \left(\frac{2 \alpha+2 \beta}{2}\right) \cos \left(\frac{2 \alpha-2 \beta}{2}\right)$ 
= $2 \cos (\alpha+\beta) \cos (\alpha-\beta)$ ....(i)
But $\sin \alpha+\sin \beta$ = 0 and $\cos \alpha+\cos \beta$ = 0
$\Rightarrow$ $\sin \alpha= -\sin \beta$ and $\cos \alpha=-\cos \beta$ .....(ii)
$\therefore$ $\cos (\alpha-\beta)$ = $\cos \alpha \cdot \cos \beta+\sin \alpha \cdot \sin \beta$ = $-\cos ^{2} \beta-\sin ^{2} \beta$ = $-\left(\cos ^{2} \beta+\sin ^{2} \beta\right)$ = -1 [using (ii)]
$\Rightarrow$ $\cos (\alpha-\beta)$ = -1 ....(iii)
Now from (i) and (iii) we have $\cos 2 \alpha+\cos 2 \beta$ = $-2 \cos (\alpha+\beta)$

Q2. If $\cot \frac{A}{2}=\frac{b+c}{a}$ then $\Delta ABC$ is
    (A) right angled   <-- CORRECT
    (B) isosceles
    (C) equilateral
    (D) Scalene
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Given $\cot \frac{A}{2}=\frac{b+c}{a}$ 
Now using sine rule a = k sin A, b = k sin B, c = k sin C
$\Rightarrow$ $\cot \frac{A}{2}$ = $\frac{k \sin B+k \sin C}{k \sin A}$ 
$\Rightarrow$ $\cot \frac{A}{2}$ = $\frac{2 \sin \left(\frac{B+C}{2}\right) \cdot \cos \left(\frac{B-C}{2}\right)}{2 \sin \frac{A}{2} \cdot \cos \frac{A}{2}}$ 
$\Rightarrow$ $\cot \frac{A}{2}$ = $\frac{2 \sin \left(\frac{\pi}{2}-\frac{A}{2}\right) \cdot \cos \left(\frac{B-C}{2}\right)}{2 \sin \frac{A}{2} \cdot \cos \frac{A}{2}}$ [$\because$ A + B + C = $\pi$ in a triangle]
$\Rightarrow$ $\frac{\cos \frac{A}{2}}{\sin \frac{A}{2}}$ = $\frac{\cos \left(\frac{B-C}{2}\right)}{\sin \frac{A}{2}}$
$\Rightarrow$ $\cos \frac{A}{2}$ - $\cos \left(\frac{B-C}{2}\right)$ = 0
$\Rightarrow$ A + B - C = 0 or A - B + C = 180^o 
Solving we get C = 90^o or B = 90^o 
Hence we get $\Delta ABC$ is a right angled triangle.

Q3. In a $\Delta ABC$ , tan $\frac A2 = \frac 56$ and $\frac C2 = 25,$ then
    (A) a, b, c are in H.M
    (B) a, b, c are in A.P.   <-- CORRECT
    (C) a, b, c are in G.P.
    (D) a, b, c are in H.P
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Given $\tan \frac{A}{2}=\frac{5}{6}$ and $\tan \frac{C}{2}=\frac{2}{5}$
But we have $\tan \frac{A}{2} \cdot \tan \frac{C}{2}=\sqrt{\frac{(s-b)(s-c)}{s(s-a)}} \cdot \sqrt{\frac{(s-a)(s-b)}{s(s-c)}}$
$\therefore \frac{5}{6} \cdot \frac{2}{5}=\frac{(s-b)}{s}$
$ \Rightarrow s=3\left( s-b \right) $
$ \Rightarrow 2\left( \frac { a+b+c }{ 2 } \right) =3b$ $\left[\because 2s=a+b+c\quad \right] $
$ \Rightarrow $ a + c = 2b
$ \Rightarrow $ a, b, c are in A.P

Q4. In a triangle ABC, the line joining the circumcentre and the incentre is parallel to BC, then cos B + cos C =
    (A) $\frac 32$
    (B) $\frac 34$
    (C) 1   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $\frac 12$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Using the given condition we have r = R cos A which gives r/R = cos A.hence Cos A + Cos B + cos C -1 = cos A which gives cos B + cos C = 1.

Q5. The value of tan 1° tan 2° tan 3° ... tan 89° is
    (A) 0
    (B) $\infty $
    (C) 1   <-- CORRECT
    (D) not defined
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Since tan 1 .tan 89 = 1...... only tan 45 in the middle which has the value 1. hence the product is 1.

Q6. If sin 2 $\theta $ = cos 3 $\theta $, then $\theta $ is equal to $\left( {0 \le \theta \le {{90}^ \circ }} \right)$
    (A) 18^o
    (B) 36^o
    (C) 18 or 90^o   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 45^o
    Answer: C
    Explanation: When the value of $θ$ is replace by 18 both LHS and RHS are equal since 38 and 54 are complimentary angles.

Q7. When the length of the shadow of a pole is equal to the height of the pole, then the elevation of source of light is
    (A) ${45^o}$   <-- CORRECT
    (B) ${75^o}$
    (C) ${60^o}$
    (D) ${30^o}$
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Consider a triangle ABC right angled at B. 
Let AB represent the pole then we have BC is it's shadow where angle C = $\theta$ is the elevation of the sun.
Then $tan \theta = \frac {AB} {BC} = 1,$ since the length of the shadow of pole = height of the pole
$ \Rightarrow tan\theta =tan{ 45 }^{ \circ }\\ \Rightarrow \theta ={ 45 }^{ \circ }$

Q8. In a $\Delta ABC,B = \frac \pi 3$ and C $ = \frac \pi 4$. Let D divide BC internally in the ratio 1 : 3, then $\frac{\sin (\angle B A D)}{\sin (\angle C A D)}=$
    (A) $\frac 13$
    (B) $\sqrt \frac 23$
    (C) $\frac {1}{\sqrt 3 }$
    (D) $\frac{1}{\sqrt{6}}$   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: In $\triangle$ABC given $\angle$ B = $\frac { \pi }{ 3 } $, $\angle $C = $\frac { \pi }{ 4 }$
Since D divide BC in the ratio 1 : 3 we can take BD = x and DC = 3x
Let AD = y
In $\triangle $CAD, using Sine Rule we get,
$\frac { AD }{ sin\angle C } = \frac { DC }{ sin\angle CAD } $
$\Rightarrow \frac { y }{ sin\frac { \pi }{ 4 } } = \frac { 3x }{ sin\angle CAD }$
$\Rightarrow \frac { y }{ \frac { 1 }{ \sqrt { 2 } } } = \frac { 3x }{ sin\angle CAD }$
$\Rightarrow sin\angle CAD=\frac { 3x }{ \sqrt { 2 } y } $ ...(i)
In $\triangle$ADB, using Sine Rule we get,
$\frac { AD }{ sin\angle B } = \frac { BD }{ sin\angle BAD } $
$\Rightarrow \frac { y }{ sin\frac { \pi }{ 3 } } = \frac { x }{ sin\angle BAD }$
$\Rightarrow \frac { y }{ \frac { \sqrt { 3 } }{ 2 } } = \frac { x }{ sin\angle BAD }$
$ \Rightarrow sin\angle BAD=\frac { \sqrt { 3 } x }{ 2y } $ ...(ii)
From (i) and (ii), we get
$\frac { sin\angle BAD }{ sin\angle CAD } =\frac { 3x }{ \sqrt { 2 } y } .\frac { 2y }{ \sqrt { 3 } x } =\frac { 1 }{ \sqrt { 6 } } $

Q9. The maximum value of ${\rm{sin}}\alpha + {\rm{ sin}}\beta + {\rm{ sin}}\gamma $ , where $\alpha ,{\rm{ }}\beta ,{\rm{ }}\gamma $ are positive real numbers satisfying $\alpha {\rm{ }} + \beta {\rm{ }} + \gamma {\rm{ }} = {\rm{ }}\pi $ is
    (A) zero
    (B) negative
    (C) (- 3)
    (D) positive   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Since the sine ratio is positive in both first and second quadrants and the angles sum up to 180 and the ratios are related to the triangle, the value is always positive.

Q10. If the sides a, b, c of a triangle ABC are in A.P., then
    (A) $2 \sin \frac{A}{2} \sin \frac{B}{2}=\sin \frac{C}{2}$
    (B) $2 \sin \frac{A}{2} \sin \frac{C}{2}=\sin \frac{B}{2}$   <-- CORRECT
    (C) $\sin \frac{A}{2} \sin \frac{B}{2}=2 \sin \frac{C}{2}$
    (D) $\sin \frac{B}{2} \sin \frac{C}{2}=\sin \frac{A}{2}$
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Since a, b, c are in AP, b = $\frac{(a+c)}{2}$ which gives a + c = 2b. adding b on both sides we get a + b + c - 2b = b. Hence 2s - 2b = b (since a + b + c = 2s) 
On simplification we have $\frac{2(s-b)}{b}$ = 1. Rewriting this equation. Using the semi sum formulae we have
$2 √(((s-b ))/b ) √(((s-b ))/b ) √(((s-c ) (s-a))/ac )$$= √(((s-c ) (s-a))/ac )$ 
$2√(((s-b) (s-c))/bc ) √(((s-a) (s-b))/ab ) $ $= √(((s-c) (s-a))/ca )$ which is the given answer.

Q11. If the sides of a triangle ABC are given as 4, 5, 7 respectively then cot $\frac B2 \cot \frac C2$ equals
    (A) $\frac {16} {9}$
    (B) 2   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 3
    (D) 1
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Since the sides of a triangle ABC are 4, 5, 7 respectively we can take a = 4x, b = 5x, c = 7x.
Then, 2s = a + b + c = 16x $\Rightarrow$ s = 8x
Now $\tan \frac{B}{2} \cdot \tan \frac{C}{2}$ = $\sqrt{\frac{(s-a)(s-c)}{s(s-b)}} \cdot \sqrt{\frac{(s-a)(s-b)}{s(s-c)}}$ = $\frac{(s-a)}{s}$
$\Rightarrow$ $\tan \frac{B}{2} \cdot \tan \frac{C}{2}$ = $\frac{(s-a)}{s}=\frac{4 x}{8 x}=\frac{1}{2}$
$\Rightarrow$ $\cot \frac{B}{2} \cdot \cot \frac{C}{2}$ = 2

Q12. If sin x - cos x =$\sqrt 2 $, then x = ( n is any integer)
    (A) 2n$\pi $
    (B) $2n\pi -\frac {\pi } { 4}$
    (C) $2n\pi \pm \pi-\frac {\pi } {4}$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) (2n + 1)$\pi $
    Answer: C
    Explanation: $\sin x-\cos x=\sqrt{2}$
Dividing throughout by $\sqrt{2}$, we get
$\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}} \sin x-\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}} \cos x=1$
$\Rightarrow \sin \frac{\pi}{4} \sin x-\cos \frac{\pi}{4} \cos x=1$
$\Rightarrow \cos \left(x+\frac{\pi}{4}\right)=-1$
$\Rightarrow \cos \left(x+\frac{\pi}{4}\right)=\cos \pi$
$\Rightarrow x+\frac{\pi}{4}=2 n \pi \pm \pi$
$\Rightarrow x=2 n \pi \pm \pi-\frac{\pi}{4}, n \in Z$

Q13. The general value of $\theta $ satisfying sin$\theta$ = -$\frac 12$ and tan $\theta $ = $\frac {1} {\sqrt 3 }$ is
    (A) $n \pi+\frac{\pi}{6}, n \in I$
    (B) $2 n \pi+\frac{11 \pi}{6}, n \in I$
    (C) $n \pi+(-1)^n \frac{7 \pi}{6}, n \in I$
    (D) $2n\pi + \frac {7\pi }{6}$, $n \in I$   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: $\sin \theta=\frac{-1}{2}$
$\Rightarrow \sin \theta=\sin \left(-\frac{\pi}{6}\right)$
$\Rightarrow \theta=n \pi+(-1)^{n}\left(-\frac{\pi}{6}\right), n \in Z$
$\Rightarrow \theta=\frac{7 \pi}{6}, \frac{11 \pi}{6}$
$\tan \theta=\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}}$
$\Rightarrow \tan \theta=\tan \left(\frac{\pi}{6}\right)$
$\Rightarrow \theta=n \pi+\left(\frac{\pi}{6}\right), n \in Z$
$\Rightarrow \theta=\frac{\pi}{6}, \frac{7 \pi}{6}$
So principal value for both $\sin \theta$ and $\tan \theta$ is $\frac{7 \pi}{6}$
Hence the general solution is $2 n \pi+\frac{7 \pi}{6}, n \in I$

Q14. The equation sin $x - \frac \pi 2 + 1$ = 0 has no root in the interval
    (A) $\left(\frac{\pi}{2}, \pi\right)$
    (B) $\left(\frac{\pi}{2}, \frac{3 \pi}{2}\right)$
    (C) $\left(0, \frac{\pi}{2}\right)$
    (D) none of these   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Since sin x lies between -1 to 1 hence the given equation will lie between $\frac{(-\pi)}{2}$ to 2 - $\frac{\pi}{2}$.

Q15. The number of values of x satisfying the condition sin x + sin 5 x = sin 3x in the interval [0, $\pi $] is
    (A) 10
    (B) 0
    (C) 6   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 2
    Answer: C
    Explanation: sin x = sin 5x = sin 3x [$\because$ sin C + sin D = $2 \sin \left(\frac{C+D}{2}\right) \cos \left(\frac{C-D}{2}\right)$]
$\Rightarrow$ 2 sin 3x cos (-2x) - sin 3x = 0
$\Rightarrow$ sin 3x [2 cos 2x - 1] = 0
$\Rightarrow$ sin 3x = 0 or, cos 2x = $\frac{1}{2}$ 
$\Rightarrow$ sin 3x = sin 0 or cos 2x = cos $\frac{\pi}{3}$ 
$\Rightarrow$ 3x = $n \pi+(-1)^{n} .0$ $\Rightarrow$ x = $\frac{n \pi}{3}$ or 2x = $2 n \pi \pm \frac{\pi}{3}$ $\Rightarrow$ x = $n \pi \pm \frac{\pi}{6}, n \in Z$ 
$\Rightarrow$ x = $0, \frac{\pi}{3}, \frac{2 \pi}{3}, \pi$ or x = $\frac{\pi}{6}, \frac{5 \pi}{6}$ 
$\Rightarrow$ 6 solutions in the interval [0, $\pi$].

Mathematical Induction Test 01  (id 617) [FREE]

Q1. Let $P\left( n \right)$ be a statement and let $P\left( n \right) \Rightarrow P\left( {n + 1} \right)$ for all natural numbers n, then what will the nature of $P\left( n \right)$ ?
    (A) it satisfies only all n $>$ 1
    (B) It is true for all n > m, m being a fixed positive integer
    (C) true for all n
    (D) Nature cannot be identified.   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Since the statement is conditional statement ( if....then).

Q2. Let P(n) b e a statement 2^n < n!, where n is a natural number, then P(n) is true for
    (A) all n > 2
    (B) all n > 3   <-- CORRECT
    (C) all n < 3
    (D) all n
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Since for example n = 4 will give LHS as 16 and RHS as 4! = 1 $\times$ 2 $\times$ 3 $\times$ 4 = 24

Q3. If x > -1, then the statement (1 + x)^n > 1 + nx is true for
    (A) all n > 1
    (B) all n > 1 provided x $ \ne $ 0   <-- CORRECT
    (C) all n $ \in $ N
    (D) all n > 2
    Answer: B
    Explanation: For example, if x = 0 and n > 1, say n = 2 then the given inequality will become 1^2 > 1, which is not true. Hence both the conditions on x and n are needed.

Q4. The smallest +ve integer n, for which n! < $\left(\frac{n+1}{2}\right)^n$ holds is
    (A) 2   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 1
    (C) 4
    (D) 3
    Answer: A
    Explanation: When n = 2, LHS: n! = 2! = 2 $\times$ 1 = 2
RHS: $(\frac{3}{2})^2= \frac{9}{4}$ 
But if n = 1, the inequation does not hold good.

Q5. If P(n) = 2 + 4 + 6 +………………..+ 2n, n $ \in $ N, then P(k) = k(k + 1) + 2 $ \Rightarrow $ P (k + 1) = (k + 1) (k + 2) + 2 for all k $ \in $ N. So we can conclude that P(n) = n (n + 1) + 2 for
    (A) all n $ \in $ N
    (B) n > 1
    (C) n > 2
    (D) nothing can be said   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Because the statement is incomplete without the conclusion.

Q6. (x^n - y^n) is divisible by (x - y) for
    (A) all n $ \in $ N   <-- CORRECT
    (B) n > 1
    (C) n > 2
    (D) n > 3
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Replacing n by 1, 2, 3... we get the expression with the factor (x - y). Hence it is divisible by (x - y).

Q7. The greatest positive integer, which divides n (n + 1) (n + 2) (n + 3) for all n $ \in $ N , is
    (A) 2
    (B) 120
    (C) 24   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 6
    Answer: C
    Explanation: If n = 1 then the statement becomes 1 $\times$ 2 $\times$ 3 $\times$ 4 = 24: the consecutive natural numbers when substituted will be multiples of 24.

Q8. Let P(n) denote the statement n^2 + n is odd, It is seen that P(n) $ \Rightarrow$ P(n + 1), therefore P(n) is true for all
    (A) n > 2
    (B) n > 1
    (C) none of these   <-- CORRECT
    (D) n
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Since if n = 1 the answer is 2 which is even. Also p(1) = p(2) is also not true since p(2) is 6 which is even.

Q9. The greatest positive integer, which divides (n + 1)(n + 2)(n + 3) ......(n + r) $\forall n \in W$, is
    (A) r
    (B) n + r
    (C) r!   <-- CORRECT
    (D) (r + 1)!
    Answer: C
    Explanation: If n = 0 the given expression becomes 1 $\times$ 2 $\times$ 3 $\times$ 4........r = r!. Also when n = 1 one more extra term will be there in the product 2 $\times$ 3 $\times$ 4........(r + 1) which is also divisible by r!.

Q10. The statement P(n) : 1$\times$1! + 2$\times$2! + 3$\times$3! + .....+ n$\times$n! = (n + 1)! - 1 is
    (A) true for all n > 2
    (B) true for all n > 1
    (C) not true for any n
    (D) true for all n $ \in $ N   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Replace n = 1, 2, 3 then LHS = RHS

Q11. A student was asked to prove a statement P(n) by method of induction. He proved that P(3) is true and that P(n) $ \Rightarrow $ P(n + 1) for all natural numbers n. On the basis of this the criteria applied on n for P(n) to be true
    (A) for no n
    (B) for all n $≥$ 2
    (C) for all n $ \in $ N
    (D) for all n $≥$ 3   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Since by the principle of mathematical induction if p(n) is true for n = 3 then that will be the minimum point to be considered for the process. Also that P(n) $\Rightarrow$ P(n + 1).

Q12. 3^2n+2 - 8^n - 9 is divisible by 64 for all
    (A) n $ \in $ N, n $>$ 3
    (B) n $ \in $ N, n $>$ 2
    (C) n $ \in $ N   <-- CORRECT
    (D) n $ \in $ N, n $≥$2
    Answer: C
    Explanation: When n = 1 the value is 64. By induction process the consecutive replacement of n = 2, 3, 4.... will be multiples of 64.

Q13. The inequality n! > 2^n-1 is true
    (A) for no n $ \in $ N
    (B) for all n$ $> 2, $n ∈N$   <-- CORRECT
    (C) for all n > 1
    (D) for all n $ \in $ N
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Since when n = 1, we get the inequality as 1 > 1, which is not true. Also for n = 2, we get 2 > 2, which is false. Hence the given statement is true for n > 2.

Q14. The statement 3^n > 4n is true for all
    (A) n > 1, n $ \in $ N
    (B) n > 2, n $ \in $ N   <-- CORRECT
    (C) n > 3, n $ \in $ N
    (D) n $ \in $ N
    Answer: B
    Explanation: 3^n > 4n for all n > 1 can be proved by induction.

Q15. n^2 + 3n is always divisible by which number, provided n is an integer?
    (A) 4
    (B) 2   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 5
    (D) 3
    Answer: B
    Explanation: P(n) = n^2 + 3n
P(1) = 1 + 3
P(1) = 4
Let’s assume that P(k) is true and divisible by 4. Therefore, P(k) = k^2 + 3k can be written as 4c.
We need to check if P(k + 1) is divisible by 4
P(k+1) = (k + 1)^2 + 3(k + 1)
P(k+1) = k^2 + 1 + 2k + 3k + 3
P(k+1) = k^2 + 5k + 4
P(k+1) = (k^2 + 3k) + 2k + 4
P(k+1) = 4c + 2k + 4
P(k+1) = 4c + 2(k + 2)
Clearly the second part of the equation is not divisible by 4. However P(k) = 4c is divisible by 2 and
P(k + 1) is also divisible by 2. Therefore, 2 divides P(n).

    (A) 3

    (C) 2
    (D) 1

Q13. If n is a +ve integer, then ${3.5^{2n + 1}} + {2^{3n + 1}}$ is divisible by
    (A) 24
    (B) 32
    (C) 64
    (D) 17   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: When n = 1 the value is 391 which is divisible by 17.

Q14. If n is a +ve integer, then 7^2n - 4 is divisible by
    (A) 2937
    (B) 3297
    (C) 2397   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 2973
    Answer: C

Q15. Let P(k) = 1 + 3 + 5 + …………….+ (2k - 1) = (3 + k^2). Then which of the following is true?
    (A) P(k) $\Rightarrow $ P(k + 1)   <-- CORRECT
    (B) P (1) is correct
    (C) P(k) $ \Rightarrow $P(k + 3)
    (D) P(k) $ \Rightarrow $P(k + 2)
    Answer: A
    Explanation: P(k) = 1 + 3 + ... + (2k - 1) = 3 + k^2 .....(i)
When k = 1, LHS of p(k) $\ne$ R.H.S of P(k)
So P(1) is not ture.
Now P(k + 1); 1 + 3 + 5 + .... + (2k - 1) + (2k + 1)
= 3 + (k + 1)^2 .....(ii)
Let P(k) is true 
$\therefore$ 1 + 3 + 5 + ..... + (2k - 1) = k^2 + 3
$\Rightarrow$ 1 + 3 + 5 + .... + (2k - 1)
= 3 + k^2 + 2k + 1 = (k + 1)^2 + 3
$\Rightarrow$ P(k + 1) true 
$\therefore$ P(k) $\Rightarrow$ P(k + 1)

Q1. The inequality 2^n + 1 < n! is true for:
    (A) all n > 1
    (B) all n > 2
    (C) all n > 3   <-- CORRECT
    (D) all n
    Answer: C
    Explanation: When n = 1, we get 3 < 1, and when n = 2, we get 5 < 2. When n = 3, we get 9 < 6, which are invalid inequations. Only when n = 4 we get 17 < 24, which is valid.

Q2. For each $n \in N$, a^2n-1 + b^2n-1 is divisible by:
    (A) (a + b)^4
    (B) a + b   <-- CORRECT
    (C) (a + b)^3
    (D) (a + b)^2
    Answer: B
    Explanation: When n = 1 we have a + b and the subsequent substitution of n as 2, 3,... will result in the expression whose factor is a + b.

Q3. For each n $ \in $ N, n (n + 1) (2n + 1) is divisible by :
    (A) 6   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 8
    (C) 15
    (D) 2
    Answer: A
    Explanation: When n = 1 the value is 6. The subsequent substitution will give the value as a multiple of 6.

Q4. For each n $ \in $ N, 3(5^2n + 1) + 2^3n + 1 is divisible by :
    (A) 23
    (B) 19
    (C) 17   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 21
    Answer: C
    Explanation: When n = 1 we have 391 which is divisible by 17.

Q5. For each n $ \in $ N, 2^3n - 1 is divisible by :
    (A) 8
    (B) 7   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 16
    (D) 19
    Answer: B
    Explanation: When n = 1 the value is 7.

Q6. For each n $ \in $ N, 3^2n - 1 is divisible by :
    (A) 32
    (B) 19
    (C) 8   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 16
    Answer: C
    Explanation: when n = 1 the value is 8 which is the multiple of 8.

Q7. The statement P(n) : (n + 3)^2 > 2^n+3 is true for:
    (A) no n $ \in $ N   <-- CORRECT
    (B) all n
    (C) all n $≥$ 3
    (D) all n $≥$ 2
    Answer: A
    Explanation: When n = 1 we get 16 > 16, which is false. when n = 2 we get 25 > 32, which is false as well. As n = 3, 4, 5.... the inequality does not hold correct.

Q8. The smallest positive integer n for which 2^n(1 $\times$ 2 $\times$ 3 $\times$ ... $\times$ n) < n^n holds is :
    (A) 4
    (B) 6   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 3
    (D) 5
    Answer: B
    Explanation: When n = 1, we get 2 < 1, which is not valid. When n takes values 2, 3, 4, 5 the inequality is invalid. But when n = 6 we have 46080 < 46656, which is valid.

Q9. If ${10^n} + 3 \times {4^{n + 1}} + k$ is divisible by 9 for all n $ \in $ N , then the least positive integral value of k is :
    (A) 3
    (B) 7
    (C) 1
    (D) 5   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: When n = 1 we have 58. Given that the expression is divisible by 9 . Hence the least value of k will be 5,so that the value becomes 63 which is divisible by 9.

Q10. The smallest positive integer n, for which (1 $\times$ 2 $\times$ 3 $\times$ ... $\times$ n) < $\left(\frac{n+1}{2}\right)^n$ holds:
    (A) 4
    (B) 3
    (C) 5
    (D) 2   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: When n = 1 we get 1 < 1, which is invalid. When n = 2 we get 2 < $\frac{9}{4}$, which is valid.

Q11. Let P(n) : n^2 - n + 41 is a prime number. Then :
    (A) P(1) is not true
    (B) P(3) is not true
    (C) P (41) is not true   <-- CORRECT
    (D) P(5) is not true
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Since when n = 41 we have (41)^2, which is not a prime number.

Q12. Let that P(n) $\Rightarrow$ P(n + 1) for all natural numbers n. Also, if P (m) is true, m $\in $ N, then we conclude that
    (A) P ( n ) is true for all n
    (B) P (n) is true for all n $ ≥$ m   <-- CORRECT
    (C) P ( n ) is true for all n $<$ m
    (D) P ( n ) is true for all n > m
    Answer: B
    Explanation: This criteria is from the basic principle of mathematical induction.

Q13. Consider the statement P(n) : n^2 $ \ge$ 100. Here P(n) $\Rightarrow$ P(n + 1) for all natural numbers n. What does it mean
    (A) P(n) is true for all n $≥$ 3
    (B) none of these   <-- CORRECT
    (C) p(n) is true for all n
    (D) P (n) is true for all n $≥ $ 2
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Since the statement is not true for n = 1, we cannot predict the validity for all natural numbers n.

Q14. The smallest positive integer ‘ n ‘ for which P ( n ) : ${2^n} < \left( {1 \times 2 \times 3 \times ............ \times n} \right)$ holds is :
    (A) 2
    (B) 1
    (C) 3
    (D) 4   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Since the values of n as 1,2,3gives inavlid inequations but when n = 4 we have $16 <24$, which is valid.

Q15. If x^n - 1 is divisible by x – k for all n belongs to natural numbers N, then the least positive integral value of k is:
    (A) 2
    (B) 1   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 3
    (D) 4
    Answer: B
    Explanation: If f(x) = x^n - 1 is divisible by x - k
Then f(k) = 0
Therefore, k^n = 1 and thus least positive integral value of k is 1.

Mathematical Induction Test 04  (id 620) [PAID]
CBSE > Class 11 > Mathematics > Principle of Mathematical Induction (Deleted)
Questions: 15 | Answers: YES

Q1. A student was asked to prove a statement P ( n ) by method of induction . He proved P ( k + 1 ) is true whenever P ( k ) Is true for all k ≥ 5 , $k \in N$ and P ( 5 ) is true . On the basis of this he could conclude that P ( n ) is true
    (A) for all $n \in \mathbf{N}$
    (B) for all n $<$ 5
    (C) for all n $≥$ 5   <-- CORRECT
    (D) for all n $>$ 5
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Yes because if it is true for n = k then it is true for all n ,which is the basic concept of mathematical induction.

Q2. If a, b, c $\in$ N, a^n + b^n is divisible by c, when n is odd but not when n is even, then the value of c is :
    (A) a + b   <-- CORRECT
    (B) a - b
    (C) a^3 + b^3
    (D) a^3 - b^3
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Since a + b will be a factor of a^n + b^n.

Q3. 1.2.3 + 2.3.4 + 3.4.5 + ………..up to n terms is equal to $\frac{1}{4}$n (n + 1) (n + 2) (n + 3) is true for
    (A) for all real number n
    (B) all natural numbers n   <-- CORRECT
    (C) whole numbers n
    (D) for all integers n
    Answer: B
    Explanation: By the method of mathematical induction the given statement is true for n = 1, 2, 3.....

Q4. 1 + 2 + 3 + .................. n = $\frac{1}{2} ( n ( n +1))$, is true
    (A) only for n < 2
    (B) for all natural numbers n   <-- CORRECT
    (C) for n = 2 only
    (D) only for n > 2
    Answer: B
    Explanation: By the process of mathematical induction, the given statement is true for all natural numbers n.

Q5. The sum of the terms in the n^th bracket of the series 1 + (2 + 3 + 4) + (5 + 6 + 7 + 8 + 9)…. is
    (A) (n + 1)^3 + 8n^2
    (B) (n + 1)^3 + 4n^2
    (C) $\frac{(n+1)(n+2)}{6 n}$
    (D) (n - 1)^3 + n^3   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: For n = 1, we have
Sum of the terms in first bracket = 1 and, (n − 1)^3 + n^3 = (1 − 1)^3 + 1^3 = 1
For n = 2, we have
Sum of the terms in the second bracket = 2 + 3 + 4 = 9 and, (n − 1)^3 + n^3 = (2 − 1)^3 + 2^3 = 1 + 8 = 9
For n = 3, we have
Sum of the terms in the third bracket = (5 + 6 + 7 + 8 + 9) = 35 and, (3 − 1)^3 + 3^3 = 8 + 27 = 35

Q6. For all $n \in N$, 2.4^2n + 1 + 3^3n + 1 is divisible by :
    (A) 5
    (B) 11   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 7
    (D) 209
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Now, for n = 1,
2.4^2 + 1 + 3^3 + 1 = 2.4^3 + 3^4 
= 2 $\times$ 64 + 81 = 128 + 81 = 209,
for n = 2,
2.4^5 + 3^7 
= 8 $\times$ 256 + 2187 = 2048 + 2187 = 4235
Note that the H.C.F. of 209 and 4235 is 11. So, 2.4^2n + 1 + 3^3n + 1 is divisible by 11.

Q7. If P (n) : 49^n +16^n + $\lambda$ is divisible by 64 for n $\in$ N is true, then the least negative integral value of $\lambda$ is
    (A) -1   <-- CORRECT
    (B) -2
    (C) -3
    (D) -4
    Answer: A
    Explanation: When n = 1 we have the value of the expression as 65 . Given that the expression is divisible be 64. Hence the value is -1.

Q8. $\frac{3}{4}+\frac{15}{16}+\frac{63}{64}+...$ to n terms is equal to
    (A) $n+\frac{4^{-n}}{3}+\frac{1}{3}$
    (B) $n-\frac{4^n}{3}-\frac{1}{3}$
    (C) $n+\frac{4^{-n}}{3}-\frac{1}{3}$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $n+\frac{4^n}{3}-\frac{1}{3}$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: $\frac{3}{4}+\frac{15}{16}+\frac{63}{64}+\ldots .$ to n terms
= $\left(1-\frac{1}{4}\right)+\left(1-\frac{1}{16}\right)+\left(1-\frac{1}{64}\right)$ + .... to n terms
= n - $\frac{1}{4}\left(1+\frac{1}{4}\right.\left.+\frac{1}{4^{2}}+\ldots . . \text { to } \mathrm{n} \text { terms }\right) $ 
= $n-\frac{1}{4} \cdot \frac{1-\left(\frac{1}{4}\right)^{n}}{1-\frac{1}{4}}$ 
= $n-\frac{1}{3}\left(1-4^{-n}\right)$

Q9. 10^2n - 1 + 1 $\forall n \in N$ is divisible by :
    (A) 7
    (B) 11   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 3
    (D) 2
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Put n = 1 we get 11, hence it is divisible by 11.

Q10. For all odd positive integers n, the number $n({n^2} - 1)$ is divisible by :
    (A) 16
    (B) 24   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 9
    (D) 36
    Answer: B
    Explanation: When n = 1 we have 0 : When n = 2 we have 24. When n = 3 we have 120 .....where all these values are multiples of 24.

Q11. $\frac{\frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{2}{2}}{1^3}+\frac{\frac{2}{2} \cdot \frac{3}{2}}{1^3+2^3}+\frac{\frac{3}{2} \cdot \frac{4}{2}}{1^3+2^3+3^3}+\ldots$ upto n terms is equal to
    (A) $\frac{n-1}{n-2}$
    (B) $\frac{n}{n+1}$   <-- CORRECT
    (C) $\frac {n}{n + 2}$
    (D) $\frac{n+1}{n+2}$
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Replace n = 1 we have $\frac{ 1}{2}$. When n = 2 we have $\frac{1}{2} + \frac{1}{6} = \frac{2}{3}$, (by the principle of mathematical induction when n = 1 we need to take one term from LHS. and for n = 2 we need to take the sum of two terms from the LHS. ....)

Q12. $\frac{1}{3.5}+\frac{1}{5.7}+\frac{1}{7.9}+\ldots$ up to n terms is equal to
    (A) $\frac{n}{(2 n+3)}$
    (B) $\frac{1}{(n+2)(n+4)}$
    (C) $\frac{1}{(n+3)(n+6)}$
    (D) $\frac{n}{3(2 n+3)}$   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: By the process of mathematical induction when n = 1 we have $\frac{1}{15} $.
When n = 2 we have LHS : $\frac{1}{15} + \frac{1}{35} = \frac{2}{21}$, RHS :$\frac{2}{(3( 4+3))}= \frac{2}{21}$, which is true.

Q13. The nth terms of the series 4 + 14 + 30 + 52 + 80 + 114 + ….. is =
    (A) 5n - 1
    (B) 2n^2 + 2n
    (C) 3n^2 + n   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 2n^2 + 2
    Answer: C
    Explanation: When n = 1 we get 4. When n = 2 we get 12 + 2 = 14...

Q14. The product of three consecutive natural numbers is divisible by
    (A) 3
    (B) 11
    (C) 8
    (D) 6   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: By replacing n by 1 we get 6.

Q15. For $n \in N$, 3^2n + 2 - 2^3n - 9 is divisible by
    (A) 3
    (B) 64   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 81
    (D) 9
    Answer: B
    Explanation: We have,
3^2n + 2 − 2^3 $\cdot$ n − 9
= (3^2)^n+1 − 8n − 9
= (1 + 8)^n + 1 − 8(n + 1) − 1
={1 + (n + 1)8+ ^n+1C_2 ​8^2 + ^n+1C_3 ​8^3 +..... + ^n+1C^n+1 ​8^n+1} − 8(n + 1) − 1
= 64 {^n+1C_2 ​+ ^n+1C_3 ​$\cdot$ 8 +......}, which is divisible by 64.

Q1. For all positive integers n, the number 4^n + 15n - 1 is divisible by :
    (A) 36
    (B) 9   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 16
    (D) 24
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Replace n = 1 we get 18 n = 2 we get 45.... By the principle of mathematical induction it is divisible by 9.

Q2. For all $n \in N$, 7^2n - 48n - 1 is divisible by :
    (A) 26
    (B) 25
    (C) 2304   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 1234
    Answer: C
    Explanation: When n = 1 the value is 0. When n = 2 the value is 2304..... Hence by the principle of mathematical induction the expression is divisible by 2304.

Q3. For all $n \in N$ 5^2n - 1 is divisible by :
    (A) 25
    (B) 11
    (C) 24   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 26
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Replace n = 1, 2, 3.. the answers are divisible by 24.

Q4. For all $n \in N$, 3^2n + 7 is divisible by :
    (A) 9
    (B) 3
    (C) 8   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 11
    Answer: C
    Explanation: When n = 1 the value is 16 which is a multple of 8, n = 2 the value is 88 which is a multiple of 8..... By PMI the expression is divisible by 8 for all natural numbers.

Q5. For all $n \in N$, 3n^5 + 5n^3 + 7n is divisible by :
    (A) 15   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 3
    (C) 5
    (D) 10
    Answer: A
    Explanation: For n = 1 the value is 15. For n = 2 the value is 150 which is a multiple of 15... By PMI the expression is divisible by 15.

Q6. The nth terms of the series 3 + 7 + 13 + 21 +………. is =
    (A) n + 2
    (B) 4n + 1
    (C) 4n - 1
    (D) n^2 + n + 1   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Put n = 1 we have 3. When n = 2, 4 + 2 + 1 = 7...

Q7. The nth terms of the series 4 + 14 + 30 + 52 + 80 + 114 + ….. is =
    (A) 5n - 1
    (B) 2n^2 + 2n
    (C) 3n^2 + n   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 2n^2 + 2
    Answer: C
    Explanation: When n = 1 we get 4. When n = 2 we get 12 + 2 = 14...

Q8. 3 + 13 + 29 + 51 + 79 +……….. to n terms is
    (A) n^2 + 4n^3
    (B) n^2 + 5n^3
    (C) 2n^2 + 7n
    (D) n^3 + 2n^2   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: When n = 1 we have 3 when n = 2 we have
LHS : 3 + 13 = 16 
RHS : 8 + 8 = 16 ..... By PMI this is the sum of the n terms of the series.

Q9. The sum of n terms of the series 1^3 + 3^3 + 5^3 + ...... is
    (A) n^2(n^2 + 1)
    (B) n^2(n^2 - 1)
    (C) n^2(2n^2 + 1)
    (D) n^2(2n^2 - 1)   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: When n = 1 we get 1. When n = 2 we get 
RHS : 4 $\times$ 7 = 28 
LHS : 1 + 27 = 28...... By PMI this is true.

Q10. If n is an odd positive integer, then a^n + b^n is divisible by:
    (A) a + b   <-- CORRECT
    (B) a^2 + b^2
    (C) a^2 - b^2
    (D) a - b
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Since when n = 1 we get a + b. Hence by the method of mathematical induction, it is true.

Q11. If n is an even positive integer, a^n + b^n is divisible by :
    (A) a - b
    (B) a^2 + b^2
    (C) none of these   <-- CORRECT
    (D) a + b
    Answer: C
    Explanation: If n is even we get a^2 + b^2 which is not divisible by a + b. same case when n = 4.

Q12. For all $n \in N, \frac{n^5}{5}+\frac{n^3}{3}+\frac{7n}{15 }$ is
    (A) a negative fraction
    (B) a natural number   <-- CORRECT
    (C) a positive fraction
    (D) an integer
    Answer: B
    Explanation: a natural number

Q13. The sum of n terms of the series 1 + (1 + a) + (1 + a + a^2) + (1 + a + a^2 + a^3)....., is
    (A) $-\frac{n}{1-a}+\frac{a\left(1-a^n\right)}{(1-a^2)}$
    (B) $\frac{\mathrm{n}}{1-\mathrm{a}}+\frac{\mathrm{a}\left(1+\mathrm{a}^{\mathrm{n}}\right)}{(1-\mathrm{a})^2}$
    (C) $-\frac{n}{1-a}+\frac{a\left(1-a^n\right)}{(1-a)^2}$
    (D) $\frac{n}{1-a}-\frac{a\left(1-a^n\right)}{(1-a)^2}$   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Replace n = 1 we get 1. Replace n = 2 we get LHS; a + 2 RHS : a + 2.

Q14. If 3 + 5 + 9 + 17 + 33 +……. to n terms = 2^n+1 + n - 2, then n^th term of LHS is
    (A) 3n - 1
    (B) 2n + 1
    (C) 2^n + 1   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 2n - 1
    Answer: C
    Explanation: 3 = 2^1 + 1
5 = 2^2 + 1 
9 = 2^3 + 1........ the nth term is 2^n + 1

Q15. $\frac{(n+2) !}{(n-1) !}$ is divisible by:
    (A) 11
    (B) 6   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 26
    (D) 24
    Answer: B
    Explanation: When n = 1 we have 3! = 6 and n = 2 we have 4! = 24 which is divisible by 6.

Quadratic Equations Test 01  (id 622) [FREE]
CBSE > Class 11 > Mathematics > Complex Numbers and Quadratic Equations
