Q1. If the integers m and n are chosen at random between 1 and 100, then the probability that a number of the form 7^m + 7^n is divisible by 5 is
    (A) $\frac{3}{4}$
    (B) $\frac{1}{7}$
    (C) $\frac{1}{5}$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $\frac{1}{4}$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: We know that 7^k, K$\in$ N, has 1, 3, 9, 7 at the unit's place for k = 4p, 4p - 1, 4p - 2, 4p - 3 respectively, where p = 1, 2, 3,..... Clearly, 7^m + 7^n will be divisible by 5 if 7^m has 3 or 7 in the unit's place and 7^n has 7 or 3 in the unit's place or 7^m has 1 or 9 in the unit's place and 7^n has 9 or 1 in the unit's place.
Thus, for any choice of m,n the digit in the unit's place of 7^m = 7^n is 2, 4, 6, 0 or 8. It is divisible by 5 only when this digit is 0.
Hence, required probability = $\frac{1}{5}$

Q2. The probability that the length of a randomly chosen chord of a circle lies between $\frac{2}{3}$ and $\frac{5}{6}$ of it's diameter is
    (A) $\frac{5}{6}$
    (B) $\frac{5}{12}$
    (C) $\frac{1}{4}$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $\frac{1}{16}$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: If l is the length of a chord, r, the distance of the mid-point of the chord from the centre of the circle and a radius of the given circle, then 
r = a cos $\theta$, l = 2a sin $\theta$ 
Given: $\frac{2}{3} 2 a<2 a \sin \theta<\frac{2}{3} 2 a$ 
$\Rightarrow$ $\frac{5}{6} a<a \cos \theta<\frac{\sqrt{5}}{3} a$ 
$\Rightarrow$ $\frac{\sqrt{11}}{6}<r<\frac{\sqrt{5}}{3} a$ 
Thus the given condition is satisfied if the mid-point of the chord lies within the region between the concentric circles of radius $\frac{\sqrt{11}}{6}$ and $\frac{\sqrt{5}}{3}$. 
Hence, the required probability = $\frac{\text { The area of the circular annulus }}{\text { Area of the given circle }}$ 
= $\left(\frac{5}{9}-\frac{11}{36}\right)=\frac{1}{4}$

Q3. The letters of the word ORIENTAL are arranged in all possible ways. The chance that the consonants and vowels occur alternately is
    (A) $\frac{1}{35}$   <-- CORRECT
    (B) $\frac{1}{70}$
    (C) $\frac{2}{35}$
    (D) $\frac{3}{35}$
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Consonants can be placed in 4! ways.
Vowels can be placed in between consonants in 4! ways
$\therefore$ Total no. of ways in which the consonants and vowels occur alternatively = $2 \times 4! \times 4!$ 
Also, total no. of ways in which the letters of the word 'ORIENTAL' can be arranged = 8!
$\therefore$ Required probability = $ \frac{{2 \times 4! \times 4!}}{{8!}} = \frac{1}{{35}}$

Q4. 8 coins are tossed at a time. The probability of getting atleast 6 heads up is
    (A) $\frac{1}{64}$
    (B) $\frac{57}{64}$
    (C) $\frac{37}{256}$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $\frac{229}{256}$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Let A be the event getting atleast 6 heads up, then, 
n(A) = $ \frac{{8!}}{{6! \times 2!}} + \frac{{8!}}{{7! \times 1!}} + 1$ [Using permutation of like letters]
= 37
Also, Total no. of outcomes = 2^8 = 256
$\therefore$ Required probability = $\frac{{37}}{{256}}$

Q5. There are 2 boxes. One box contains 3 white balls and 2 black balls. The other box contains 7 yellow balls and 3 black balls. If a box is selected at random and from it, a ball is drawn, the probability that the ball drawn is black is
    (A) $\frac{1}{20}$
    (B) $\frac{1}{5}$
    (C) $\frac{1}{3}$
    (D) $\frac{7}{20}$   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Let E_1 = selecting b on I
E_2 = selecting b on II
A = drawing a black ball
$\therefore$ P(E_1) = P(E_2) = $\frac{1}{2}$ 
Now, $P\left( {\frac{A}{{{E_1}}}} \right) = \frac{2}{5},P\left( {\frac{A}{{{E_2}}}} \right) = \frac{3}{{10}}$ 
$\therefore$ By law of total probabiliy,
$P(A) = P({E_1}).P\left( {\frac{A}{{{E_2}}}} \right) + P({E_2}).P\left( {\frac{A}{{{E_1}}}} \right)$ 
= $ \frac{1}{2} \times \frac{2}{5} + \frac{1}{2} \times \frac{3}{{10}} = \frac{7}{{20}}$

Q6. If A and B are two mutually exclusive events, then P (A + B) is equal to
    (A) P(A) P(B’) + P(A’) P(B)
    (B) P(A) P(B)
    (C) P (A) P (B’) P(A’) P (B)
    (D) P(A) + P(B)   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: For two mutually exclusive events A and B, we have $A \cap B = \phi $ i.e. there is no common elements exist between them, then we have from addition theorem of probability, P(A or B) = P(A $ \cup$ B) = P(A + B) = P(A) + P(B) - P(A $ \cap $ B) = P(A) + P(B) [as P(A $ \cap $ B) = 0]

Q7. If ${E_1}$ and ${E_2}$ are two independent events, then P $\left( {{E_1} \cap {E_2}} \right)$ is equal to
    (A) $P\left( {{E_1})P({E_2}} \right)$   <-- CORRECT
    (B) $P\left( {{E_1}) + P({E_2}} \right) + P({E_1} + \cup {E_2})$
    (C) $P\left( {{E_1})-\;P\;({E_2}} \right)$
    (D) $P\left( {{E_1})+\;P\;({E_2}} \right)$
    Answer: A
    Explanation: We have, $P({E_1} \cap {E_2}) = P({E_1}).P(\frac{{{E_2}}}{{{E_1}}})$
Since E_1 and E_2 are independents, therefore
$P = \left( {\frac{{{E_2}}}{{P({E_1}) = P({E_2})}}} \right)$
$\therefore P({E_1} \cap {E_2}) = P({E_1}).P({E_2})$

Q8. If E is an event, then P $\left( {\bar E} \right)$ is equal to
    (A) 1 – P (E)   <-- CORRECT
    (B) P (E)
    (C) 1 + P (E)
    (D) - P (E)
    Answer: A
    Explanation: We have, $(E \cup \overline E ) $ = S
$\therefore P(E \cup \overline E )$ = P(S)
$ \Rightarrow P(E) + P(\overline E ) = P(S)$ {Using addition therom and E and $\overline E $ are mutually exclusive}
$\Rightarrow P(E) + P(\overline E ) = 1$ {Probability in sample space is unque}
$\Rightarrow P(\overline E ) $ = 1 - P(E)

Q9. The probability that a teacher will give an unannounced test during any class is $\frac 15$. If a student is absent twice, then the probability that he misses atleast one test is
    (A) $\frac 4 5$
    (B) $\frac 25$
    (C) $\frac {9} {25}$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $\frac {7}{25}$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Required probability = 1 - P[student does not miss any test]
= 1 - P[teacher does not take a test when the student was absent]
= $1 - \left[ {1 - \frac{1}{5}} \right] \times \left[ {1 - \frac{1}{5}} \right]$ 
= $1 - \frac{4}{5} \times \frac{4}{5} = 1 - \frac{{16}}{{25}}$ 
= $\frac{9}{{25}}$

Q10. If the probability for A to fail in an examination is 0.2 and that for B is 0.3, then the probability that either A fails or B fails is
    (A) 0.44   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 0.5
    (C) 0.06
    (D) 0.08
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Required probability = 1 - P[neither a fails nor B fails]
= 1 - P[A does not fail] x [B does not fail]
= 1 - 0.8 x 0.7
= 0.44

Q11. In a single throw of two dice, the probability of getting a total of 7 or 9 is
    (A) $\frac{4} {18}$
    (B) $\frac{1}{ 3}$
    (C) $\frac{7} {18}$
    (D) $\frac{5} {18}$   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: A = getting a total of 7
B = getting a total of 9
Then, A = {(6,1), (1, 6), (4, 3), (3, 4)}
B = {(6, 3), (3, 6), (5, 4), (4, 5)}
$\therefore$ Required probability ${ = \;P(A\; \cup \;B)}$
= P(A) + P(B)
$=\frac{4}{6^2}+\frac{4}{6^2}=\frac{8}{6^2}=\frac{8}{36}=\frac{4}{18}$

Q12. Three numbers are chosen at random without replacement from {1,2,3,.......,10}.The probability that the minimum of the chosen numbers is 3,or their maximum is 7,is
    (A) 3/40
    (B) 5/40
    (C) $7/40$
    (D) 11/40   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Let A(B) denote the event that minimum (maximum) number on the chosen tickets is 3(7).
We have,
P(A)=P(choosing 3 and two other numbers from 4 to 10)
=$\frac{^7C_2}{^{10}C_3}=\frac{7}{40}\\ $.
P(B)=P(choosing 7 and choosing two other numbers from 1 to 6)=$\frac{^6C_2}{^{10}C_3}=\frac{1}{8}=\frac{5}{40}$.
$P(A\cap B)=$P(choosing 3 and 7 and one other number from 4 to 6)
=$\frac{3}{^{10}C_3}=\frac{1}{40}\\ $
$\therefore P(A\cup B)=P(A)+P(B)-P(A\cap B)$
=7/40+5/40-1/40=11/40.

Q13. Let A and B be two independent events. The probability that both A and B occur is $\frac {1}{12}$ and the probability that neither A nor B occurs is $\frac 12$. The respective probabilities of A and B are
    (A) $\frac 1 6$ and $\frac 1 2$
    (B) $\frac{1}{3}$ and $\frac{1}{4}$ or $\frac{1}{4}$ and $\frac{1}{3}$   <-- CORRECT
    (C) $\frac 12$ and $\frac{1}{6}$
    (D) none of thse
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Since A & B are independents and therefore $\bar A $ & $\bar B$ are independent
$\therefore P(A \cap B) = P(A)P(B)$ and $P(\overline A \cap \overline B ) = P(\overline A )\,.\,P(\overline B )$ 
$\Rightarrow P(\bar A \cdot P(\bar B) = \frac{1}{12}$......(1)
$P(\bar A )\cdot P(\bar B ) = \frac{1}{2}$ 
$ \Rightarrow [1 - P(A)][1 - P(B)] = \frac{1}{2}$ $\Rightarrow 1 - P(B) - P(A) + P(A)\cdot P(B) = \frac{1}{2}$ 
$\Rightarrow 1 - P(B) - P(A) + \frac{1}{{12}} = \frac{1}{{2}}$ ...(By (1))
$\Rightarrow P(B) = \frac{7}{{12}} - P(A)$ ...(2)
$\therefore$ From (1), $P(A)\left[ {\frac{7}{{12}} - P(A)} \right] = \frac{1}{{12}}$ 
$\Rightarrow$ 12P(A)^2 - 7P(A) + 1 = 0
$\Rightarrow $ (4(P(A) - 1) (3P(A) - 1) = 0
$\Rightarrow $ P(A) = $\frac{1}{4}$ or P(A) = $\frac{1}{3}$ 
When $P(A) = \frac{1}{4}(1)$ gives $P(B) = \frac{1}{3}$ and when $P(A) = \frac{1}{3},(1)$ gives $P(B) = \frac{1}{4}$

Q14. Two events A and B have probabilities 0.25 and 0.50 respectively. The probability that both A and B occur simultaneously is 0.14. Then the probability that neither A nor B occurs is
    (A) 0.89
    (B) 0.39   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 0.375
    (D) 0.86
    Answer: B
    Explanation: We have, P(A) = 0.25, P(B) = 0.50, $P(A \cap B) $ = 0.14
$\therefore$ Required Probability $= P(\overline A \cap \overline B )$
$= 1 - P(A \cap B)$
= 1 - [P(A) + P(B) - P(A $\cap$ B)]
= 1 - [0.25 + 0.50 - 0.15]
= 0.39

Q15. India plays two matches each with West Indies and Australia. In any match the probabilities of India getting points 0, 1 and 2 are 0.45, 0.5 and 0.5 respectively. Assuming that the outcomes are independent, the probability of India getting atleast 7 points is
    (A) $\frac {1}{16}$
    (B) 0.525
    (C) 0.1125
    (D) 0.8750   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Here, there are total 5 ways by which India can get at least 7 points.
2 points + 2 points + 2 points + 2 points =$(0.5 \times 0.5 \times 0.5 \times 0.5)$
1 point + 2 points + 2 points + 2 points = $(0.05 \times 0.5 \times 0.5 \times 0.5)$
2 points + 1 point + 2 points + 2 points $=(0.5 \times 0.05 \times 0.5 \times 0.5)$
2 points + 2 points + 1 point + 2 points =$(0.5 \times 0.5 \times 0.05 \times 0.5)$
P(atleast 7 points) = $0.5 \times 0.5 \times 0.5 \times 0.5+4[0.05 \times 0.5 \times 0.5 \times 0.5]$
= 0.0625 + 4(0.00625)
= 0.0625 + 0.025
= 0.0875

Probability Test 04  (id 680) [PAID]
CBSE > Class 11 > Mathematics > Probability
Questions: 15 | Answers: YES

Q1. An unbiased dice is rolled four times. The probability that the minimum number on any toss is not less than 3 is
    (A) $\frac{80}{81}$
    (B) $\frac{16}{81}$   <-- CORRECT
    (C) $\frac{65} {81}$
    (D) $\frac{1}{81}$
    Answer: B
    Explanation: The probability that the outcome of a single throw of a die is any one of 4, 3, 4, 5 and 5 is equal to $\frac{4}{6} = \frac{2}{3}$ of the die is rolled four times, the required probability is equal to
${\left( {\frac{2}{3}} \right)^4} = \frac{{16}}{{81}}$

Q2. The probability that a man will live for 10 more years is $\frac 14$ and that his wife will live 10 more years is $\frac 13$. The probability that neither will be alive in 10 years is
    (A) $\frac {5} {12}$
    (B) $\frac {7}{12}$
    (C) $\frac {11}{12}$
    (D) $\frac 12$   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Probability that a man will not live 10 more years $= 1 - \frac{1}{4} = \frac{3}{4}$
Probability that a man will not live for 10 more years $= 1 - \frac{1}{3} = \frac{2}{3}$
$\therefore$ Required probability $= \frac{3}{4} \times \frac{2}{3} = \frac{1}{2}$

Q3. The probability of having atleast one tail in five throws with a coin is
    (A) $\frac{1} {32}$
    (B) 1
    (C) $\frac{1}{5}$
    (D) $\frac{31} {32}$   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Required probability = 1 - P [not getting any tail in five throws with a coin]
$ = 1 - \frac{1}{2} \times \frac{1}{2} \times \frac{1}{2} \times \frac{1}{2} \times \frac{1}{2}$ 
$= 1 - \frac{1}{{{2^6}}} = 1 - \frac{1}{{32}} = \frac{{31}}{32}$

Q4. If E is any event associated with an experiment, then range of P(E).
    (A) P (E) $ \le $ 0
    (B) P (E) $ \ge $ 0
    (C) $0 \le P\;(E) \le 1$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) P (E) $ \ge $1
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Let A be the event associated with a random experiment. Let m be the no. of cases favourable to the event A and n be the total no. of outcomes.
Then, we have,
$P(A) = \frac{m}{n}$
Clealry, $0 \leqslant m \leqslant n.$ 
Therefore, $0 \leqslant \frac{m}{n} \leqslant 1$
$\Rightarrow 0 \leqslant P(A) \leqslant 1$

Q5. A and B toss 3 coins. The probability that they both obtain the same number of heads is
    (A) $\frac{1}{ 9}$
    (B) $\frac{3} {16}$
    (C) $\frac{5}{16}$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $\frac{3}{ 8}$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Required probability = P [A gets no head and B gets no head] + P [A gets 1 head and B gets 1 head] + P [A gets 2 heads and B gets 2 heads] + P [A gets 3 heads and B gets 3 heads]
$= \frac{1}{8} \times \frac{1}{8} + \frac{3}{8} \times \frac{3}{8} + \frac{3}{8} \times \frac{3}{8} + \frac{1}{8} \times \frac{1}{8}$ 
$= \frac{{1 + 9 + 9 + 1}}{{64}} = \frac{{20}}{{64}} = \frac{5}{{16}}$

Q6. Three identical dice are rolled. The probability that the same number will appear on each of them is
    (A) $\frac{1}{36}$   <-- CORRECT
    (B) $\frac{1}{6}$
    (C) $\frac{1}{18}$
    (D) $\frac{3}{28}$
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Since throwing a single die three times is equivalent to throw three dice at a time.
$\therefore $ Sample space = {(1, 1, 1), (2, 2, 2), (3, 3, 3), (4, 4, 4), (5, 5, 5), (6, 6, 6), .......}
Here, n(5) = 6^3
$\therefore $ Required Probability $= \frac{6}{{{6^3}}} = \frac{1}{{{6^2}}} = \frac{1}{{36}}$

Q7. Suppose n $( \ge {\rm{3}})$ persons are sitting in a row. Two of them are selected at random. The probability that they are not together is
    (A) $1 - \frac{2}{n}$   <-- CORRECT
    (B) $\frac{2}{ n} - 1$
    (C) n!
    (D) $n - \frac{1}{n}$
    Answer: A
    Explanation: n persons can be arranged in n! ways. If any two persons are together, then this may be arranged in (n - 1)! 2! ways.
Now the probability of two persons sitting together is = $\frac{(n-1) ! 2 !}{n !}=\frac{(n-1) ! 2 \times 1}{n(n-1) !}=\frac{2}{n} \text {. }$. Hence, the probability that the two persons are not together is = 1 - $\frac{2}{n}$

Q8. One ticket is selected at random from 100 tickets numbered 00, 01, 02…, 99. Suppose S and T are the sum and product of the digits of the number on the ticket, then the probability of getting S = 7 and T = 0 is
    (A) $\frac{1} {50}$   <-- CORRECT
    (B) $\frac{2}{19}$
    (C) $\frac{19}{100}$
    (D) $\frac{1}{ 4}$
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Given 100 tickets numbered 00, 01, 02…, 99. , S and T are the sum and product of the digits of the number on the ticket.
here the number of elements in the sample space = 100
Now, S = 7 i.e. sum of the digits is 7, S = {07, 70, 16, 61, 25, 52, 34, 43}, and T = 0 i.e. product of the digits = 0, T = {00, 01, 02, 03, 04, 05, 06, 07, 08, 09, 10, 20, 30, 40, 50, 60, 70, 80, 90}
T = 0 and S = 7 $ \Rightarrow $ T $ \cap$ S = {07, 70} $ \Rightarrow $ n(T $ \cap$ S) = 2
Hence P(T = 0 and S = 7) = P( T $ \cap$ S) = $\frac{2}{100}$ = $\frac{1}{50}$

Q9. A box contains 10 eggs out of which 4 are rotten. Two eggs are taken out together. If one of them is found to be good, the probability that other is also is
    (A) $\frac{8}{15}$
    (B) $\frac{2}{3}$
    (C) $\frac{1}{ 3}$
    (D) $\frac{5} {13}$   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Let $$E_1 = one of the two eggs is good and
E_2 = both the selected eggs are good.
Here, $E_2$ is subset of
$\therefore p(\frac {E_2}{E_1})=\frac {p(E_1\cap E_2)}{p(E_1)}$
$=\frac{p(E_2)}{p(E_1)}$$=\frac{^6C_2}{^6C_2+^6C_1.4C_3}=\frac{15}{15+24}=\frac{5}{13}$

Q10. A digit is selected at random from either of the two sets {1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 6, 7, 8, 9} and {1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 6, 7, 8, 9}. What is the chance that the sum of the digits selected is 10?
    (A) $\frac{1}{9}$   <-- CORRECT
    (B) $\frac{2}{9}$
    (C) $\frac{10}{18}$
    (D) $\frac{10}{81}$
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Let A = {1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 6, 7, 8, 9} then, n (A $\times$ A) = g^2
Let B be the event that sum of the digits is 10. Then,
B = {(1, 9), (9, 1), (4,6), (6, 4), (8, 2), (2, 8), (7, 3), (3, 7), (5, 5)}
$\therefore $ Required probability $ = \frac{{n(B)}}{{n(A \times A)}} = \frac{9}{{{9^2}}} = \frac{1}{9}$

Q11. If two squares are chosen at random on a chess board, the probability that they have a side common is
    (A) $\frac{2}{ 7}$
    (B) $\frac{1}{ 9}$
    (C) $\frac{1}{18}$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $\frac{4}{ 9}$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Total number of squares = 64.
Two squares can be selected in $^{64}C_2$ ways.
In each column, there are 7 pairs of adjacent squares where each pair share 1 side in common.
Total such pairs = 8 $\times$ 7 = 56.
In each row, there are 7 pairs of adjacent squares where each pair share 1 side in common.
Total such pairs = 8 $\times$ 7 = 56.
Therefore, favourable cases = 56 + 56 = 112.
Required probability = $\frac{112}{^{64}C_2}=\frac{112}{2016}=\frac{1}{18}$

Q12. A coin tail on both sides is tossed twice. The probability of getting a head is
    (A) $\frac{1}{2}$
    (B) $\frac{3}{ 4}$
    (C) 1
    (D) 0   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Since a coin tail has both faces as tail. So getting head is an impossible event. So its probability is zero.

Q13. The chance that an event E occurs or does not occurs
    (A) 3
    (B) 2
    (C) 1   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 0
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Let E denotes the given event. Then, $\overline E$ denotes E does not occur
We have, $E \cup \overline E = S$ 
$ \Rightarrow P(E \cup \overline E ) = P(S) = 1[\because P(S) = 1]$ 
$\therefore $ Required probability is, $P(E \cup \overline E ) = 1$

Q14. Mean number of ‘sixes’ in four throws of a fair dic is
    (A) $\frac{6}{4}$
    (B) $\frac{1}{4}$
    (C) 1
    (D) $\frac{4}{6}$   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Let X be a random variable denoting the no. of sixes. Then X follows B(4, P)
Here, p Denotes the probability of getting a six in a single throw of a die
$\therefore$ Required probability = nP
$ = 4 \times \frac{1}{6} = \frac{4}{6}$

Q15. From a deck of 52 cards, the probability of drawing a court card is
    (A) $\frac{1}{13}$
    (B) $\frac{4}{13}$
    (C) $\frac{3}{13}$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $\frac{1}{4}$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Since there are 12 court cards in a pack of 52 cards. Therefore,
Required probability = $\frac{{12}}{{52}} = \frac{3}{{13}}$

Q1. A coin is tossed 6 times. The probability of getting a head three times is
    (A) $\frac{3}{64}$
    (B) $\frac{1}{2}$
    (C) $\frac{1}{ 8}$
    (D) $\frac{5}{16}$   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Let X be a random variable denoting the no. of heads.
Then, X $ \sim$ B(6, P), Where P is the probability of getting a head in a single toss of a coin
$\therefore P = \frac{1}{2}$ 
$\therefore $Required probability = P(X = 3)
= $ {}^6{C_3}{\left( {\frac{1}{2}} \right)^3}{\left( {\frac{1}{2}} \right)^{6 - 3}}$ 
= $ \frac{6}{3} \times \frac{5}{2} \times \frac{4}{1} \times {\left( {\frac{1}{2}} \right)^6} = \frac{5}{{16}}$

Q2. Five letters are sent to different persons and addresses on the five envelopes are written at random. The probability that all the letters reach correct destiny is
    (A) $\frac{1}{5}$
    (B) $\frac{1}{120}$   <-- CORRECT
    (C) $\frac{44} {120}$
    (D) $\frac{2}{5}$
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Let A denote the event that all the letters reach the correct destiny. Then,
n(A) = 1
Also, five letters can be sent of different persons in 5! ways
$\therefore $ Required probability $ = P(A) = \frac{1}{{5!}} = \frac{1}{{120}}$

Q3. The letters are sent to different persons and addresses on the three envelopes are written at random. The chance that none of the letters reaches correct destiny is
    (A) 0
    (B) 1
    (C) $\frac{1}{3}$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $\frac{2}{ 3}$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Let A denote the event that none of the letters reaches the correct destiny. Then, n(A) = 2 as
$\begin{array}{*{20}{l}} {\;\;\;\;\;\;\;{\text{Persons}}} \\ {{\text{A}}\;\;\;\;\;\;{\text{B}}\;\;\;\;{\text{C}}} \\ { \downarrow \;\;\;\;\; \downarrow \;\;\; \downarrow } \\ {{\text{c}}\;\;\;\;\;\;{\text{a}}\;\;\;\;\;{\text{b}}\;\;\; \leftarrow \;{\text{letters}}} \\ {{\text{b}}\;\;\;\;\;{\text{c}}\;\;\;\;\;\;{\text{a}}\;\;\; \leftarrow \;{\text{letters}}} \end{array}$ 
Also, three letters can be sent of different persons in 3! ways
$\therefore $ required probability = $ P(A) = \frac{2}{{3!}} = \frac{2}{6} = \frac{1}{3}$

Q4. A pack of cards contain 4 aces, 4 kings, 4 queens and 4 jacks. Two cards are drawn at random. The probability that atleast one of them is a king is
    (A) $\frac{3}{16}$
    (B) $\frac{1}{9}$
    (C) $\frac{9}{20}$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $\frac{1}{5}$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Required probability = 1 - P [none of the two cards is a king]
$= 1 - \frac{{12{C_2}}}{{16{C_2}}}$
$ = 1 - \frac{{11}}{{20}} = \frac{9}{{20}}$

Q5. Three houses are available in a locality. Three persons apply for the houses. Each applies for one house without consulting others. The probability that all the three apply for the same house is
    (A) $\frac{4}{15}$
    (B) $\frac{1}{9}$   <-- CORRECT
    (C) $\frac{1}{4}$
    (D) $\frac{1}{3}$
    Answer: B
    Explanation: For a particular house being selected
Probability = $\frac {1}{3}$ 
Probability that all the persons apply for the same house 
= $3(\frac{1}{3}×\frac{1}{3}×\frac{1}{3})=\frac{1}{9}$

Q6. From each of the four married couples, one of the partners is selected at random. The probability that those selected are of the same sex is
    (A) $\frac{1}{4}$
    (B) $\frac{1}{2}$
    (C) $\frac{1}{8}$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $\frac{1}{16}$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Here, s = {(M M M M), (F F F F), ......}
Clearly, n (s) = 16
$\therefore $ Required proability = P [( M M M M) or (F F F F)]
= P [(M M M M) + (F F F F)]
= $\frac{2}{{16}} + \frac{2}{{16}} = \frac{4}{{16}} = \frac{1}{8}$

Q7. The probability of having atleast one tail in 4 throws with a coin is
    (A) $\frac{1}{16}$
    (B) $\frac{15} {16}$   <-- CORRECT
    (C) $\frac{1}{ 4}$
    (D) 1
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Required probability = 1 - (Probability of getting no tail in 4 throws of a coin)
= $1 - \frac{1}{{{2^4}}} = 1 - \frac{1}{{16}}$ 
= $\frac{15} {16}$

Q8. The letters of the word PROBABILITY are arranged in all possible ways. The chance that two B’s and also two I’s occur together is
    (A) $\frac{2} {55}$   <-- CORRECT
    (B) $\frac{2}{165}$
    (C) $\frac{1}{55}$
    (D) $\frac{4} {165}$
    Answer: A
    Explanation: No. of ways in which letters of the word 'PROBABILITY' can be arranges $ = \frac{{11!}}{{2! \times 2!}}$ 
Now, consider two B's as one letter and two I's as one letter.
Then no. of ways in which the letters can be arranged = 9!
$\therefore $Required probability = $ \frac{{9!}}{{\frac{{11!}}{{2! \times 2!}}}}$ 
= $\frac{{9!}}{{11!}} \times 2! \times 2! = \frac{2}{{55}}$

Q9. Two persons A and B appear in an interview for two vacancies. If the probabilities of their selection are $\frac 14$ and $\frac 16$ respectively, then the probability that none of them is selected is
    (A) $\frac {19}{12}$
    (B) $\frac {1}{24}$
    (C) $\frac 5 8$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $\frac {5}{12}$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Required probability = $P\left[ {\bar A \cap \bar B} \right]$ 
= $P\left[ {\bar A} \right].\,P\left[ {\bar B} \right]$ ($\because$ $\bar A$ and $\bar B$ are independent)
= $ [1 - P(A)].[1 - P(B)]$ 
= $\left( {1 - \frac{1}{4}} \right).\left( {1 - \frac{1}{6}} \right) = \frac{3}{4} \times \frac{5}{6}$ 
= $ \frac{5}{8}$

Q10. A fair coin is tossed n times and let X denotes the number of heads obtained. If P (X = 4), P (X = 5) and P (X = 6) are in A.P., then n is equal to
    (A) 14
    (B) 7 or 14   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 21
    (D) 7
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Since P(X = 4), P(X = 5) and P(X = 6) are in A.P, therefore,
2P(X = 5) = P(X = 4) + P(X = 6)
$\Rightarrow( 2×{ ^nC_5})(\frac{1}{2})^n= ^nC_4(\frac{1}{2})^n+^nC_6(\frac{1}{2})^n$ 
$\Rightarrow$ $2 \times^{n} C_{5}=^{n} C_{4}+^{n} C_{6}$ 
$\Rightarrow$ n = 7 or 14

Q11. A five digit number is formed by the digits 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 6 and 8. The probability that the number has even digit at both ends is
    (A) $\frac{1}{5}$
    (B) $\frac{1}{3}$
    (C) $\frac {2}{7}$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $\frac{2}{9}$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Five digits out of seven digits can be arranged in $^7P_5$ ways = 2520
There are 4 even numbers (2, 4, 6, 8).
Two can be arranged at the end in $^4P_2$ ways = 12
Remaining three places of five digit numbers can be filled with remaining 5 digits in $^5P_3$ ways = 60
So, required probability $=\frac{12×60}{2520}=\frac{2}{7}$

Q12. A sum of money is rounded of to the nearest rupee. The probability that the error occurred in rounding off is atleast 15 paise is
    (A) $\frac{71}{101}$
    (B) $\frac{29}{100}$
    (C) $\frac{71} {100}$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $\frac{29}{101}$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: The sample space is, s = {-0.50, -0.49, -0.48, ......., -0.01, 0.00, 0.01, ....... 0.49}
Let A be the event that the round of error is at least 15 paise.
Then A^c is the event that the round of error is at most 14 paise.
The sample space of A^c is, 
A^c = {-0.14, -0.15, ........, -0.01, 0.00, 0.01, ........., 0.14}
$\therefore P({A^c}) = \frac{{29}}{{100}}$ 
$\therefore P(A) = 1 - p({A^c}) = 1 - \frac{{29}}{{100}} = \frac{{71}}{{100}}$

Q13. Three numbers are chosen at random from 1 to 20. The probability that they are consecutive is
    (A) $\frac{3}{190}$   <-- CORRECT
    (B) $\frac{1}{57}$
    (C) $\frac{1}{47}$
    (D) $\frac{1}{60}$
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Total no. of elements in the sample space is ${}^{20}{C_3}$ i.e. = 1140 ways.Now as three numbers are consecutive,the Numbers may be 1, 2, 3 or 2, 3, 4 or 3, 4, 5 or .... 18,19, 20. this can be done in 18 ways. Hence the probability of getting three consecutive numbers is $\frac{18}{1140}$ = $\frac{3}{190}$

Q14. A positive integer is chosen at random. The probability that sum of the digits of its square is 39 is
    (A) 1
    (B) 0   <-- CORRECT
    (C) $\frac{1}{11}$
    (D) $\frac{2}{39}$
    Answer: B
    Explanation: There is no positive integer for which sum of square of digits is 39. So required probability is zero

Q15. A box contains 100 tickets numbered 1, 2, 3,........,100. Two tickets are chosen at random. If it is given that Maximum number on the two chosen tickets is not more than 10, then the probability that the minimum number on them is not less than 5 is,
    (A) $\frac{2}{3}$
    (B) $\frac{3}{5}$
    (C) $\frac{1}{3}$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $\frac{2}{5}$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Let A be the event, maximum number is not more than 10 and B be the event, minimum number is not less than 5
$\therefore P(A)=\frac{^{10}C_2}{^{100}C_2}\ and P(A \cap B)=\frac{^6C_2}{^{100}C_2}$ 
Required probability = P(B/A)$=\frac{P(A\cap B)}{P(B)}=\frac{^6C_2}{^{10}C_2}=\frac{1}{3}$
